Grammar and Remarks
The Participle raayih 
ɀǉȾȮƁȚȤ 
going, having gone
A participle is a word that has the characteristics of both a verb and an adjective. It behaves 
like an adjective because it has only three forms for gender and number: masculine, femi-
nine, and plural (see adjectives, lesson ). It functions like a verb in that it is usually negated 
by ma-, ma-jaalis ǏȮȾŽƾű ȼȳ (not sitting). (For negation, see lesson .) A participle can have 
an object, either a noun or an attached pronoun. The basic meaning of an active participle 
is “doing,” or “having done,” the English equivalent of an adjective or verb ending in “-ing.” 
Participle words have several variant patterns depending on the verb stem from which they 
are derived from (regular, double, hollow, or weak, see lessons  and ). Examples:
Regular: jilas 
to sit 
ǏȼȮƴȾȮű 
jaalis 
sitting, having sat 
ǏȮȾŽƾű
Double: 
dazz 
to send 
ȿȥȼȢ 
daazz 
sending, having sent 
ȿȥȚȢ
Hollow: shaaf 
to see 
ȯƾŵ 
shaayif 
seeing, having seen 
ǗȾȮƁƾŵ
Weak: 
buqa 
to stay 
ƾȼȮƲȽŮ 
baaqi 
staying, having stayed 
ǠȮŻƾȮŮ
 
For the purpose of this book we shall discuss here only the participle word raayih ɀǉȾȮƁȚȤ 
(going), which is derived from the verb stem raah ȠȚȤ (to go), probably the most and com-
monly used participle in colloquial Arabic. Below is a conjugation table of raayih showing 
its three forms: masculine, feminine, and plural. (Audio)
aani raayih 
I am going (M). 
ǉȾȮƁȚȤ Ǡſȕ
aani raayha 
I am going (F). 
ǀ ȼƇɀȮƁȚȤ Ǡſȕ
ihna raayhiin 
We are going (M). 
ƞƇɀȮƁȚȤ ƾƶŲȘ
ihna raayhaat 
We are going (F). 
ȝƾƇɀȮƁȚȤ ƾƶŲȘ
inta raayih 
You are going (M). 
ǉȾȮƁȚȤ ǁſȘ
inti raayha 
You are going (F). 
ǀ ȼƇɀȮƁȚȤ ǠƄſȘ
intu raayhiin 
You are going (MP). 
ƞȮƇɀȮƁȚȤ ǞƄſȘ
intu raayhaat 
You are going (FP). 
ȝƾƇɀȮƁȚȤ ǞƄſȘ
huwwa raayih 
He is going. 
ǉȾȮƁȚȤ Ǟƀ
hiyya raayha 
She is going. 
ǀ ȼƇɀȮƁȚȤ Ǡƀ
humma raayhiin 
They are going (M). 
ƞȮƇɀȮƁȚȤ ǝȿƵȮȽƀ
humma raayhaat 
They are going (F). 
ȝƾƇɀȮƁȚȤ ǝȿƵȮȽƀ
 
Notice that the stem vowel “i” in the masculine singular raayih drops out when a suﬃx 
beginning in a vowel is added in the feminine and plural forms. Occasionally, the forms 

At the Bank |  
of raayih are used in place of the future preﬁxes “rah-” and “ha-” to give a future meaning 
(going to). (Audio)
shwakit raayih tsaaﬁr 
il-ammaan?
When are you going to travel 
to Amman?
 ɀȲ ǍȾȮźƾƉɀȮů ǉȾȮƁȚȤ ǁȮȾżȼǞɀȮŵ
ȒȴƾȮȿƵȮȼŸ
humma raayhiin lil-bank
They are going to the bank.
ǙƶȮȼƃɀȮƴȮȾŽ ƞƇɀȮƁȚȤ ǝȿƵȮȽƀ
raayha truuh lil-beet
She is going to go home.
ǁƸƃɀȮƴȮȾŽ ȠȶǍɀȮů ǀƇɀȮƁȚȤ
ween raayhaat baachir?
Where are you going 
tomorrow?
ȒǍȾȮǩƾŮ ȝƾƇȾɀƁȚȤ ǜƁȶ
The Demonstrative Words: asmaa· il-ishaara ȜȤƾŵȁȚ ȔƾƵȮ ɀŴȖ
Iraqi Arabic has two groups of demonstrative words equivalent to the English demonstra-
tives “this/these” and “that/those.” Each group has three forms: masculine, feminine, and 
plural. Some of the words may have variants, but only the more common ones will be con-
sidered here. The demonstrative words agree with the noun in gender and number. There is 
only one plural form for both genders of each group.
This / That Group (Audio)
haadha
this (M)
ȚȣƾȮƀ
haadha 
taalib
This is a student.
ƿŽƾŶ Țȣƾƀ
haadhi
this (F)
ȸȣƾƀ
haadhi 
taaliba
This is a student.
 ȸȣƾƀ
ǀƃŽƾŶ
hadhoola
these (M/F/P)
ǝȼȮŽȶǌȮȼƀ
hadhoola 
tullaab
These are students.
 ǀŽȶǌȼƀ
țǾȽȮŶ
hadhoola 
taalibaat
These are students.
 ǝŽȶǌȼƀ
ȝƾƃŽƾŶ
That / Those Group (Audio)
hadhaak
that (M)
ȱȚǌȮȼƀ
hadhaak 
walad
That is a boy.
ǋȼȮŽȼȶ ȱȚǌȮȼƀ
hadhiich
that (F)
ǶƁǌȮȼƀ
hadhiich 
binit
That is a girl.
ǁȾȮƶȾȮŮ ǶȮƁǌƀ
hadhoolaak
those (M/F/P)
ȱǽȶǌȼƀ
hadhoolaak 
wilid
Those are boys.
ǋȮȾŽȾȶ ȱǽȶǌȼƀ
hadhoolaak 
banaat
Those are girls.
ȝƾƶȼŮ ȱǽȶǌȼƀ
 
The demonstrative words of both groups may be reduced to the short preﬁx “ha- ȼȮƀ” 
if they precede a noun deﬁnite with the article “il- ɀȮŽȘ” (the) and that is the more common 
usage. (Audio)

 |  l e s s o n  
hal-walad 
this/that boy 
ǋȮŽȼǞɀȮŽƾȮȼƀ
hal-beet 
this/that house 
ǁƸȮƃɀȮŽƾȮȼƀ
has-sayyaara 
this/that car 
ȜȤƾƸȮ ǆƉŽƾȮȼƀ
hat-taaliba 
this/that student 
ǀƃȮȾŽƾȿȮƭŽƾȮȼƀ
 
The demonstrative words are negated by placing the word “muu ǞȮȽž,” (not) before the 
demonstrative if the noun is deﬁnite and after the demonstrative if the noun is indeﬁnite. 
Notice the structural diﬀerence between the two: The ﬁrst is a demonstrative noun phrase, 
whereas the latter is an equational statement. (Audio)
muu hal-beet 
not this house 
ǁƸƃŽƾȮȼƀ ǞȮȽž
muu hadhaak il-beet 
not that house 
ǁƸȮƃɀȮŽȚ ȱȚǌȮȼƀ ǞȮȽž
hadhaak muu beet 
That is not a house. 
ǁȮƸȮŮ ǞȮȽž ȱȚǌȮȼƀ
hadhoola muu tullaab 
These are not students. 
țǾŶ ǞȮȽž ǀŽȶǌȮȼƀ
Idioms and Common Phrases (Audio)
1. maysiir ǍƸƫȮɀƸȼž 
impossible, it can’t be (invariable idiomatic word consists of “ma- 
Ȯȼž,” [not] and “ysiir ǍƸƫȮɀƁ ,” [to become])
maysiir tishrab biira 
bish-shaari
You can’t drink beer in the 
street.
 ȜǍƸȮŮ țǍɀȮƪȾů ǍƸƫȮɀƸȼž

Baabil siwiyya

Babylon together.
 ǚŮƾŮ ȯǞƪɀȮſ ȳȥǽ ǍƸƫȮɀƸȼž
ǝȮȿƁǞȮ ȾŴ

You can’t travel without a 
visa.
 ȴȶǋȾȮŮ ǍȮȾźƾƉɀȮů ǍƸƫȮɀƸȼž
ȜǎȮƸȮź
maysiir timshuun ib-nuss 
ish-shaari
You can’t walk in the middle 
of the street.
 ǑȽȮƶȮɀŮ ȴǞƪȮɀƵȮȾů ǍƸƫȮɀƸȼž
ȬȤƾȿȮƪŽȚ

ǚƸȮƴɀȮŽƾŮ ǛƀɀȤȶǎɀȮů ǍƸƫȮɀƸȼž
2. maykhaalif ǗȮȾŽƾƈȮɀƸȼž 
It is okay, it is permitted, it doesn’t matter (invariable 
idiomatic word consists of “ma-,” [not] and “ykhaalif,” [to diﬀer]; it is the opposite of maysiir)

Basma
It is okay for you (F) to go 
with Basma.
 ǝȮǆƁȾȶ ƞŲȶǍɀȮů ǗȮȾŽƾƈȮɀƸȼž
ǀƵƉŮ
maykhaalif tzuurhum 
bil-leel
It is okay for you (M) to visit 
them at night.
ǚƸȮƴɀȮŽƾŮ ǛƀɀȤȶǎɀȮů ǗȮȾŽƾƈȮɀƸȼž

Creative Dialogues
a.
taalib :
ween raayha?
ǀ ȼƇȮɀƁȚȤ ǜƁȶ  : ƿŽƾŶ
taaliba : aani raayha lil-bank
ǙƶƃɀȮƴȮȾŽ ǀ ȼƇɀƁȚȤ Ǡſȕ  : ǀƃŽƾŶ
taalib :
ayy bank?
ȒǙƶȮȼŮ ȸȖ  : ƿŽƾŶ
taaliba : bank ir-Raaﬁdeen
ǜƁǋźȚȿǍȮŽȚ ǙƶȮȼŮ  : ǀƃŽƾŶ
taalib :
shaku indich bil-bank?
ȒǙƶƃɀȮŽƾȾȮŮ ɃȾǋɀȮƶȮȾŸ ǞƳ ȼŵ  : ƿŽƾŶ
taaliba : ariid asarruf haadhi 
ish-sheekaat
ȝƾƳƸȿȮƪŽȚ ȸȣƾƀ ȯȿǍ ȼǧȖ ǋƁȤȖ  : ǀƃŽƾŶ
taalib:
rah-aji wiyyaach
ɃƾȮȿƁȾȶ ǠűȖ ɀȠȼȤ  : ƿŽƾŶ
taaliba : yalla, khalli nruuh siwiyya
ǝȮǆƁȼǞȮ ȾŴ ȠȶǍɀȮſ ǠȿȮƴȼų ȆǝǆȮƴȮȼƁ  : ǀƃŽƾŶ

 |  l e s s o n  
D R I L L S
b.
taalib :
min ween hadhoolaak 
il-banaat?
ȒȝƾƶƃŽȚ ȱǽȶǌȼƀ ǜƁȶ ǜȾž  : ƿŽƾŶ
taaliba : humma min Baghdaad
ȢȚǋưŮ ǜȾž ǝȿƵȮȽƀ  : ǀƃŽƾŶ
taalib :
wa hadhoolaak il-wilid?
ȒǋȮȾŽȾǞȮŽȚ ȱǽȶǌȼƀȶ  : ƿŽƾŶ
taaliba : humma min il-Muusil
ǚ ȾǧǞƓȚ ǜž ǝȿƵȮȽƀ  : ǀƃŽƾŶ
taalib :
sh-da-ysawwuun hnaak?
ȒȱƾƶȮɀƀ ȴȶȿǞȼȮƉȮɀƁȼǋɀȮŵ  : ƿŽƾŶ
taaliba : yzuuruun aathaar Baabil 
il-qadiima
ǀƚǋƲŽȚ ǚŮƾŮ ȤƾŰȕ ȴȶȤȶǎȮɀƁ  : ǀƃŽƾŶ
taalib :
shgadd baaqiin b-Baabil?
ȒǚŮƾŮ ɀț ƞŻƾŮ ǋȮƳɀȮŵ  : ƿŽƾŶ
taaliba : humma baaqiin yoom waahid
ǋȾŲȚȶ ȳǞƁ ƞȮŻƾŮ ǝȿƵȮȽƀ  : ǀƃŽƾŶ
taalib :
wenn humma saakniin?
ȒƞƶɀȮżƾŴ ǝȿƵȮȽƀ ǜƁȶ  : ƿŽƾŶ
taaliba : humma saakniin b-madiinat 
il-Hilla 
ǀȮƴƑȚ ǀƶƁǋȼž ɀț ƞƶżƾŴ ǝȿƵȮȽƀ  : ǀƃŽƾŶ

DARIS ARBAATAASH  ǐȮȼƯȼȮŶƾŮɀȤȖ ȦȾȤȼȢ
At the Post Oﬃce
bil-bariid 
 ǋȮƁǍȮȼƃɀȮŽƾȾȮŮ
Basma goes to the post oﬃce to buy stamps. She walks toward one window and asks the 
man behind it,
Basic Dialogue (Audio)
. Basma: haadha shubbaach it-tawaabi?
Is this the stamp window?
ȒǕŮȚǞȿȮƭŽȚ ɃƾȮȿƃȽȮŵ Țȣƾƀ :ǀƵƉŮ
. muwazzaf: naam, w haadha shubbaach 
il-hawaalaat il-maaliyya.
Yes, and that is the window of money 
orders.
 ȝǽȚǞ ȼƇɀȮŽȚ ɃƾȿƃȽȮŵ Țȣƾƀȶ ȆǛƯſ :ǗȿȮŷȼǞȽž
.ǀƸŽƾƵɀȮŽȚ
. Basma: min fadlak, ariid tawaabi 
l-Amriika.
Please, I want stamps to America.
.ƾƳƁǍɀžȖ ɀȲ ǕŮȚǞȼȮŶ ǋƁȤȖ ȆǙƴƬź ǜȾž :ǀƵƉŮ
. muwazzaf: triidiin twaabi risaala loo 
bostkart?
Do you want stamps for a letter or a 
postcard?
 ǞŽ ǀŽƾŴȾȤ ǕŮȚǞŶ ǜƁǋƁǍů :ǗȿȮŷȼǞȽž
ȒȝȤƾƳ ɀŴǞŮ
. Basma: ariid twaabi risaala.
I want stamps for a letter.
.ǀŽƾŴȾȤ ǕŮȚǞŶ ǋƁȤȖ :ǀƵƉŮ
. muwazzaf: risaala bil-bariid ij-jawwi loo 
il-aadi?
Air or regular mail letter?
ȒȸȢƾƯŽȚ ǞŽ ȸȿǞǆƐȚ ǋƁǍȼƃɀȮŽƾŮ ǀŽƾŴȾȤ :ǗȿȮŷȼǞȽž

 |  l e s s o n  
. Basma: kam yoom bil-bariid ij-jawwi?
How many days by airmail?
ȒȸȿǞȮǆƐȚ ǋƁǍȼƃɀȮŽƾŮ ȳǞƁ ǛȼȮż :ǀƵƉŮ
. muwazzaf: usbuu bil-bariid ij-jawwwi, 
w-usbuueen bil-aadi.

 ƞŸǞƃ ɀŴȽȚȶ ȸȿǞǆƐȚ ǋƁǍƃŽƾŮ ȬǞƃ ɀŴȽȚ :ǗȿȮŷȼǞȽž
.ȸȢƾƯŽƾŮ
. Basma: bil-bariid ij-jawwi, alla 
ykhalliik. shgadd siir it-tawaabi?
Airmail, please. How much are the stamps?
 .ǙƸȿȮƴȼȮƈɀƁ ʇȚ ȆȸȿǞȿƐȚ ǋƁǍƃŽƾŮ :ǀƵƉŮ
ȒǕŮȚǞȿȮƭŽȚ ǍȾƯ ȾŴ ǋȼȮƳ ȼŵ
. muwazzaf: nuss diinaar.
One-half of a dinar.
.ȤƾƶƁȢ ǑȽȮſ :ǗȿȮŷȼǞȽž
. Basma: ween aku sanduuq bariid?
Where is a mail box?
ȒǋƁǍȮȼŮ ȰȶǋɀȮƶ ȼǧ ǞżȖ ǜƁȶ :ǀƵƉŮ
. muwazzaf: hnaak.
Over there.
.ȱƾƶɀƀ :ǗȿȮŷȼǞȽž
. Basma: shukran jaziilan.
Many thanks.
.ǾƁǎű ȹȚǍƳŵ :ǀƵƉŮ
. muwazzaf: fwan.
Don’t mention it.
.ȹȚǞɀȮƱȮȼŸ :ǗȿȮŷȼǞȽž

Grammar and Remarks
The Verb chaan / ykuun ȴǞƳȮɀƁ / ȴƾǩ 
was, were/will
The Iraqi chaan is a hollow verb and its use corresponds to the verb “to be” in English in 
the past tense, “was/were,” but not in the present tense “is/are/am.” The present form ykuun 
is used to convey future tense and usually comes with the future tense preﬁx “rah-.” Notice 
that the Iraqis do not use the consonant “ch” of the past tense chaan with the present form 
kuun. Below is a conjugation table of the two forms. (Audio)
Past Tense: chaan ȴƾȮǩ
chaan naayim 
He was sleeping. 
ǛȾȮƁƾſ ȴƾȮǩ
chaanat naayma 
She was sleeping. 
ǀȼƵȮɀƁƾſ ǁȼȮſƾȮǩ
chaanaw naaymiin 
They were sleeping (M). 
ƞȮƵȮɀƁƾſ ɀǞȼȮſƾȮǩ
chaanaw naaymaat 
They were sleeping (F). 
ȝƾ ɀƚƾſ ɀǞȼȮſƾȮǩ
chinit naayim 
You were sleeping (M). 
ǛȮȾȮƁƾſ ǁȾȮƶȾȮǩ
chinti naayma 
You were sleeping (F). 
ǀȼƵȮɀƁƾſ ǠƄɀȮƶȾȮǩ

 |  l e s s o n  
chintu naaymiin 
You were sleeping (MP). 
ƞȮƵȮɀƁƾſ ǞƄɀȮȮƶȾȮǩ
chintu naaymaat 
You were sleeping (FP). 
ȝƾƵȮɀƁƾſ ǞƄȮɀȮƶȾȮǩ
chinit naayim 
I was sleeping (M). 
ǛȾȮƁƾſ ǁȾȮƶȾȮǩ
chinit naayma 
I was sleeping (F). 
ǀȼƵȮɀƁƾſ ǁȾȮƶȾȮǩ
chinna naaymiin 
We were sleeping (M). 
ƞƵȮɀƁƾſ ƾȿȮƶȾȮǩ
chinna naaymaat 
We were sleeping (F). 
ȝƾƵȮɀƁƾſ ƾȿȮƶȾȮǩ
Present / Future Tense: ykuun ȴǞƳȮɀƁ
ykuun naayim 
He will be sleeping. 
ǛȾȮƁƾſ ȴǞƳȮɀƁ
tkuun naayma 
She will be sleeping. 
ǀƵȮɀƁƾſ ȴǞƳȮɀȮů
ykuunuun naaymiin 
They will be sleeping (M). 
ƞȮƵȮɀƁƾſ ȴǞſǞƳȮɀƁ
ykuunuun naaymaat 
They will be sleeping (F). 
ȝƾƵȮɀƁƾſ ȴǞſǞƳȮɀƁ
tkuun naayim 
You will be sleeping (M). 
ǛȾȮƁƾſ ȴǞƳɀȮů
tkuuniin naayma 
You will be sleeping (F). 
ǀƵȮɀƁƾſ ƞſǞƳɀȮů
tkuunuun naaymiin 
You will be sleeping (MP). 
ƞȮƵȮɀƁƾſ ȴǞſǞƳȮɀȮů
tkuunuun naaymaat 
You will be sleeping (FP). 
ȝƾƵȮɀƁƾſ ȴǞſǞƳȮɀȮů
akuun naayim 
I will be sleeping (M). 
ǛȾȮƁƾſ ȴǞżȖ
akuun naayma 
I will be sleeping (F). 
ǀƵȮɀƁƾſ ȴǞżȖ
nkuun naaymiin 
We will be sleeping (M). 
ƞƵȮɀƁƾſ ȴǞƳɀȮſ
nkuun naaymaat 
We will be sleeping (F). 
ȝƾƵȮɀƁƾſ ȴǞƳɀȮſ
 
The form chaan is also used as an auxiliary followed by a present verb or by a participle 
and in this case it modiﬁes the time of the verb. (Audio)
chaanaw saakniin biiid
They were living far away.
ǋƸƯȾȮŮ ƞƶɀȮżƾŴ ɀǞȼȮſƾȮǩ
chaanat tudrus arabi
She was studying Arabic.
ǠŮǍŸ ȦȽȤɀǋȽȮů ǁȼȮſƾȮǩ
aani chinit juuaan
I was hungry.
ȴƾŸǞű ǁȾȮƶȾȮǩ Ǡſȕ
chintu laazim tudursuun 
bil-maktaba
You (P) had to study in the 
library.
ǀƃƄȮƳƓƾŮ ȴǞŴɀȤȽǋȽȮů ȳȥǽ ǞƄɀȮƶȾȮǩ
chaanat idha sayyaara
She used to have a car.
ȜȤƾȿȮƸ ȼŴ ƾƀɀǋȮȾŸ ǁȼȮſƾȮǩ
baachir tkuun ahsan, 
inshaalla
Tomorrow you will be 
better, God willing.
ʇƾƪſȘ Ȇǜ ȼƉɀŲȖ ȴǞƳɀȮů ǍȾȮǩƾŮ
huwwa rah-ykuun bil-beet
He will be home.
ǁƸȮƃɀȮŽƾŮ ȴǞƳɀƁ ȠȼȤ Ǟƀ
humma ykuunuun 
msaafriin baachir
They will be traveling 
tomorrow.
ǍȾȮǩƾŮ ǜƁǍɀȮźƾƉɀž ȴǞſǞƳɀƁ ǝȿƵȮȽƀ
 
The verb chaan / ykuun is negated with the word “ma- ȼȳ.” (Audio)
ma chaanaw saakniin biiid

ǋƸƯȾȮŮ ƞƶɀȮżƾŴ ɀǞȼȮſƾȮǩ ȼȳ
aani ma chinit juuaan
I was not hungry.
ȴƾŸǞű ǁȾȮƶȾȮǩ ȼȳ Ǡſȕ

humma ma-ykuunuun 
msaafriin baachir
They will not be traveling 
tomorrow.
 ǜƁǍɀȮźƾƉɀž ȴǞſǞƳɀƁ ȼȳ ǝȿƵȮȽƀ
ǍȾȮǩƾŮ
huwwa ma rah-ykuun bil-beet
He will not be home.
ǁƸȮƃɀȮŽƾŮ ȴǞƳɀƁ ɀȠȼȤ ȼȳ Ǟƀ
The Words il, 9ind, maal “have” and “to”
Arabic has no verb equivalent to the verb “to have” in English. In Iraqi Arabic, the mean-
ing of possession (to have) is expressed by one of the words below combined with a proper 
attached pronoun (pronoun suﬃx).
1. The Preposition Preﬁx l- 
ɀȲ “to”
The preposition takes form “ l-” or “ li- ȮȾŽ .” It precedes deﬁnite nouns and proper names, 
therefore it should not be confused with the deﬁnite article “il-” (the), which is discussed in 
lesson . (Audio)
beetak
your (M) house
ǙƄȮƸŮ
misha 
l-beetak
He went to 
your house.
 ɀȲ ƾȼȮƪȾž
ǙƄȮƸŮ
maktabich
your (F) oﬃce
ǶȮƃƄɀȮƳȼž
misha 
l-maktabich
He went to 
your oﬃce.
 ɀȲ ƾȼȮƪȾž
ǶȮƃƄɀȮƳȼž
il-mataar
the airport
ȤƾƭȼƵɀȮŽȘ
misha 
lil-mataaar
He went to 
the airport.
 ƾȼȮƪȾž
ȤƾƭȼƵɀȮƴȮȾŽ
il-ﬁndiq
the hotel
ȰȾǋɀȮƶȮȾƱɀȮŽȘ
misha 
lil-ﬁndiq
He went to 
the hotel.
 ƾȼȮƪȾž
ȰȾǋɀȮƶȮȾƱɀȮƴȮȾŽ
is-sayyaara
the car
ȜȤƾƸȮ ǆƉŽȘ
misha 
lis-sayyaara
He went to 
the car.
 ƾȼȮƪȾž
ȜȤƾƸ ǆƉȮƴȮȾŽ
Basma 
ǀƵƉŮ 
nita qalam l-Basma 
He gave a pencil to Basma. 
ǀƵƉŮ ɀȲ ǛƴŻ ƾȼȮƭȾſ
Samiir 
ǍƸƵŴ 
nita ktaab l-Samiir 
He gave a book to Samiir. 
ǍƸƵŴ ɀȲ țƾƄż ƾȼȮƭȾſ
2. The Preposition il- 
ɀȲȘ “for, belonging to/have”
It takes the form “il-” and is used with an attached pronoun or a proper name to express 
the meaning of possession (to have). This proposition preﬁx is negated with muu Ǟž (not). 
Students should not confuse the “il-” (have) with the article “il-” (the) because the former 
must always take an attached pronoun, whereas the latter precedes nouns or adjectives and 
never takes attached pronouns. (Audio)
haadha ilich
This is yours/belongs to you 
(F).
ǶȾȮŽȘ Țȣƾƀ
haadha ili w ilak
This is mine and yours/
belongs to me and you (M).
ǙȼȮŽȘȶ ǠȮȾŽȘ Țȣƾƀ
ilkum haqq
You (P) are right (lit., you 
have right).
ǘȮȼŲ ǛȽȮƳɀȮŽȘ

 |  l e s s o n  
haadha li-ktaab il-Samiira
This book belongs to Samiira.
ȜǍƸƵŴ ɀȲȘ țƾȮƄɀȮƳȾȮŽ Țȣƾƀ
haadha muu ilich
This isn’t yours (F).
ǶȾȮŽȘ Ǟž Țȣƾƀ
haadha muu ili wala ilak
This is neither mine nor 
yours (M).
ǙȼȮŽȘ ǽȶ ǠȾŽȘ Ǟž Țȣƾƀ
haadha li-ktaab muu 
il-Samiira
This book doesn’t belong to 
Samiira.
ȜǍƸƵŴ ɀȲȘ Ǟž țƾȮƄɀȮƳȮȾŽ Țȣƾƀ
3. The Preposition 9ind ǋɀȮƶȾŸ “have, at the place of”
It has two meanings or usages. One usage expresses the meaning “in the possession of,” in 
other words “to have” in English, when it is combined with an attached pronoun. Notice 
that with the pronouns for “she,” “we,” “you” (P), and “they” the consonant “n” of ind drops 
out for phonetic reasons. (Audio)
indi 
I have (MF) 
ȸǋɀȮƶȾŸ 
idna 
we have (MF) 
ƾſɀǋȮȮȾŸ
indak 
you (M) have 
ȱȼǋɀȮƶȾŸ 
idkum 
you (PM) have 
ǛȽȮżɀǋȮȾŸ
indich 
you (F) have 
ɃȾǋɀȮƶȾŸ 
idkum 
you (PF) have 
ǛȽȮżɀǋȮȾŸ
inda 
he has 
ȵȼǋɀȮƶȮȾŸ 
idhum 
they (M) have 
ǛȮȽƀɀǋȮȾŸ
idha 
she has 
ƾƀɀǋȮȾŸ 
idhum 
they (F) have 
ǛȮȽƀɀǋȮȾŸ
 
The second usage conveys the meaning “at the place of,” or “at the house of.” (Audio)
il-walad ind Saami
The boy is at Saami’s house.
ǠžƾŴ ǋɀȮƶȮȾŸ ǋŽǞŽȘ
ijeet min ind Saami gabul 
saaa
I came from Saami’s house an hour 
ago.
 ǠžƾŴ ǋɀȮƶȮȾŸ ǜȾž ǁƸű
ǀŸƾŴ ǚȽȮƃȼȮǬ
ali ind il-hallaaq
Ali is at the barber’s (place).
ȰǾȮǆƑȚ ǋɀȮƶȮȾŸ ǠƴŸ
Basma ind musallih 
is-saaaat
Basma is at the watch repairman’s 
(place).
 ǉƦȮƴ ȼƫȽž ǋɀȮƶȮȾŸ ǀƵƉŮ
ȝƾŸƾ ȿƉŽȚ
 
The preposition ind that conveys the meaning “have” is made negative with maa ƾž and 
the one that has the meaning “at the house/place of” is negated with muu Ǟž. (Audio)
maa indi sayyaara 
I don’t have a car. 
ȜȤƾȿƸȮ ȼŴ ȸǋƶȮȾŸ ƾž
il-walad muu ind Saami 
The boy is not at Saami’s house. 
ǠžƾŴ ǋƶȮȾŸ Ǟž ǋŽǞŽȘ
4. The particle maal 
ɀȲƾž “of, belonging to/have”
It is widely used in the Iraqi dialect. The particle is used in two basic phrases. One uses the 
invariable form maal in a construction consisting of two nouns or noun phrases, usually 
deﬁnite, and separated by maal. The meaning of such a phrase is expressed in the English 
language with the “of” construction or the possessive “s.” (Audio)

At the Post Oﬃce |  
il-maktaba maal ij-jaamia 
the library of the university 
ǀƯžƾȿƐȚ Ȳƾž ǀƃƄƳƓȘ
il-baab maal il-beet 
the door of the house 
ǁƸƃŽȚ Ȳƾž țƾƃŽȚ
is-sayyaara maal ali 
Ali’s car 
ǠƴŸ Ȳƾž ȜȤƾƸȮ ȿƉŽȚ
dihin maal akil 
cooking oil 
ǚżȖ Ȳƾž ǜȮȾƀȾȢ
kulliyya maal banaat 
a girls’ college 
ȝƾƶŮ Ȳƾž ǀƸȿȮƴȽȮż
 
The second phrase of the particle consists of using maal with an attached pronoun 
(pronoun suﬃx). With this construction the particle has three forms, masculine maal Ȳƾž, 
feminine maalat ǁȼȮŽƾž, and plural maalaat ȝǽƾž. The gender and the number of the pre-
ceding word determines the form of maal and its attached pronoun. In other words, the 
form maal is used after a masculine singular word; the form maalat is used after a feminine 
singular, dual, or plural word/noun not referring to human beings (inanimate); and the 
form maalaat is used after plural word/noun referring to human beings (animate).
 
Note: Although maal suﬃxed with an attached pronoun conveys the same general 
meaning as that of a noun with an attached pronoun (see lesson ), the construction of the 
two is diﬀerent—an equational sentence in the former and phrase in the latter. (Audio)
Noun with Suﬃxed maal 
Suﬃxed Noun
il-qalam maali 
ǠȾŽƾž ǛƴȼȮƲɀȮŽȘ 
qalami 
ǠƵȼȮƴȼȮŻ
The pencil is mine. 
 
my pen

ǙȼȮƄɀȮŽƾž ȜȤƾƸȮ ȿƉŽȘ

ǙȼȮůɀȤƾȿƸȮ ȼŴ

Forms of maal are negated with muu Ǟž, which is placed directly before the particle. 
(Audio)

A college is not for girls.

The pen is not mine. 
ǠŽƾž Ǟž ǛƴƲɀȮŽȘ

The watches are not yours (F).

In the Restaurant
bil-matam 
 ǛȮȼƯɀȮƭȼƵɀȮŽƾȾȮŮ
Waliid invites Basma to a special dinner of ﬁsh called Masguuf, one of the most popular 
Iraqi dishes, at a restaurant on Abu Nuwaas Street, long considered the center of Baghdad 
night life along the Tigris River.
Basic Dialogue (Audio)
. Waliid: ahibb aizmich ala aklat 
simach Masguuf.

Masguuf ﬁsh.
 ǶȼƵ ȾŴ ǀȼȮƴɀȮżȖ ǟƴŸ ǶȮȾžǎȾŸȖ ƿȾŲȖ :ǋƸŽȶ
.ȯǞǦȮ ɀƉȼž

Masguuf?

.ȢȚǋưŮ ɀț ǀȼȮƴɀȮżȖ ƿȼƸɀȮŶȖ Ǡƀ :ǋƸŽȶ

il-matam?

The two are sitting in a restaurant along “Nahar Dijla ǀƴȮɀűȾȢ ǍȮȼƷȼȮſ,” the Tigris River, and a

.ȹƾȮƯɀƃȼȮŶ ȆȯǞǦ ɀƉȼž ǃȼƵȮ ȾŴ :ǀƵƉŮ

In the Restaurant |  

Grammar and Remarks
The Preposition 9ala / 9a- ȼȮŸ / ǟƴȼŸ 
on, upon, about
ala is a common preposition in Arabic in general. In the Iraqi dialect it basically means 
“on” but also has variant meanings of “upon” and “about,” depending on the context. The 
preposition is used with nouns or attached pronouns. If the noun is indeﬁnite the complete 
form of ala is used, whereas the shortened form “a-” is used with the deﬁnite noun in 
conjunction with the article “il-” (the). (Audio)
ala kursi
on a chair
ǠŴɀǍȽȮż ǟƴŸ
al-kursi
on the chair
ǠŴɀǍȽȮƳɀȮŽȚ ȼȬ
ala meez
on a table
ǎƸž ǟƴŸ
al-meez
on the table
ǎƸƵɀȮŽȚ ȼȬ
ala jariida
on a 
newspaper
ȜǋƁǍȼű ǟƴŸ
aj-jariida
on the 
newspaper
ȜǋƁǍȿƐȚ ȼȬ
ala siiniyya
on a tray
ǀȿƸȮƶȮƸǧ ǟƴŸ
as-siiniyya
on the tray
ǀȿƸȮƶȮƸ ȿƫŽȚ ȼȬ
 
When ala is suﬃxed with an attached pronoun, it has the variant form alee- plus the 
pronoun, except with the ﬁrst person, which takes the form alayya. (Audio)

 |  l e s s o n  

2. shloon-ma thibb / ma-triid ǋƁǍɀȮů ȼȳ / ƿ ȾƇɀȮů ȼȳ ȴǞƴɀȮŵ

Drills 
tamaariin ǜƁȤƾƵȼȮů
1. Give appropriate oral replies to the following:
ween shaari Abu Nuwaas? 
ȒȦȚǞſ ǞŮȚ ȬȤƾŵ ǜƁȶ
minu izam Basma? 
ȒǀƵƉŮ ȳȼǎȮȾŸ ǞƶȮȾž
li-hsaab layya loo aleech?

DARIS SITTAASH  ǐȮȼƯȼȮƭɀȮƭ ȾŴ ȦȾȤȼȢ
Family and Relatives
ahal wa garaayib 
 ƿȾȮƁȚǍȼȮǬȶ ǚȮȼƀȖ
Basma inquires about Waliid’s family and relatives.
Basic Dialogue (Audio)
. Basma: ween tuskun?
Where do you live?
ȒǜȽȮƳ ɀƉȽȮů ǜƁȶ :ǀƵƉŮ
. Waliid: aani askun wiyya ahli 
b-Baghdaad.
I live with my family in Baghdad.
.ȢȚǋưŮ ɀț ǠƴɀƀȖ ǝȮȿƁȾȶ ǜȽȮƳ ɀŴȖ Ǡſȕ :ǋƸŽȶ
. Basma: gulli shii an ahlak.
Tell me something about your family.
.ǙȼȮƴɀƀȖ ǜȼŸ Ǡ Ⱦŵ ǠƴɀȮƴȽȮǬ :ǀƵƉŮ
. Waliid: aani mizzawwij wa indi walad 
wi-bnayya
I am married and have a boy and girl.
 .ǀȿƸȼȮƶɀȮŮȾȶ ǋȼȮŽȼȶ ȸǋɀȮƶȾŸȶ ȟƦȶǆǎȮȾž Ǡſȕ :ǋƸŽȶ
. Basma: alla ykhalliilak yyaahum.
May God keep them for you.
.ǛȽƀƾƁ ǙȼȮƴȮƸƦȮƴȮ ȼƈȮɀƁ ʇȚ :ǀƵƉŮ
. Waliid: shukran. waaldi w waalitti 
saakniin wiyyaana.
Thank you. My father and mother are 
living with us.
 ƞƶɀȮżƾŴ ǠůɀǋȮȾŽȚȶȶ ȸǋɀȮŽȚȶ .ȹȚǍƳŵ :ǋƸŽȶ
.ƾſƾȮȿƁȾȶ
. Basma: indak ikhwaan wa khawaat?
Do you have brothers and sisters?
ȒȝȚǞȼȮųȶ ȴȚǞɀȮųȘ ȱȼǋɀȮƶȾŸ :ǀƵƉŮ

 |  l e s s o n  
. Waliid: ii, indi akheen wa ukhut.
Yes, I have two brothers and one sister.
.ǁȽȮųȽȚȶ ƞƦȮųȖ ȸǋɀȮƶȾŸ ȆǠȮȾǣ :ǋƸŽȶ
. Basma: idhum atfaal?
Do they have children?
 ȒȲƾƱɀȮŶȖ ǛƀɀǋȾŸ :ǀƵƉŮ
. Waliid: ii, idhum hwaaya atfaal.
Yes, they have many children.
.ȲƾƱŶȖ ǀƁȚǞȮɀƀ ǛƀɀǋȾŸ ȆǠȮȾǣ :ǋƸŽȶ
. Basma: yani, inta amm wa khaal.
Well, you are an uncle (paternal and 
maternal).
.Ȳƾųȶ ȿǛȮȼŸ ǁſȘ ȆǠƶɀƯȮȼƁ :ǀƵƉŮ
. Waliid: wa indi amma wa khaala.
And I have aunts (paternal and 
maternal).
.ǀŽƾųȶ ǀȿƵȮȼŸ ȸǋɀȮƶȾŸȶ :ǋƸŽȶ
. Basma: ammtak wa khaaltak, kam tiﬁl 
idhum?
Your aunts, how many children do they 
have?
 ǚȾȮƱ ȾŶ Ǜż ȆǙƄŽƾųȶ ǙȼȮƄɀƵȼŸ :ǀƵƉŮ
ȒǛƀɀǋȮȾŸ
. Waliid: idhum wilid wa banaat kbaar.
They have grown boys and girls.
.ȤƾƃɀȮż ȝƾƶȼŮȶ ǋȾȮŽȾȶ ǛƀɀǋȾŸ :ǋƸŽȶ
. Basma: maashaalla, eeltak chibiira.
Praise God, your family is large.
.ȜǍƸȮƃȾȮǩ ǙȼȮƄȮɀȮƴƸȮŸ Ȇʇƾŵƾž :ǀƵƉŮ
. Waliid: naam, ilhamdu lillaa. inti 
laazim tzuuriin ahli.
Yes, thank God. You must visit my family.
 ǜƁȤȶǎɀȮů ȳȥǽ ǠƄſȘ .ʇ ȽǋɀȮƵȼƑȘ ȆǛƯſ :ǋƸŽȶ
.ǠƴɀƀȖ
. Basma: shukran, yimkin yoom ij-juma 
ij-jaayya.
Thanks, perhaps next Friday.
 ǀȼƯȮɀƵȮǇƐȚ ȳǞƁ ǜƳɀƵȾȮƁ ȆȹȚǍƳŵ :ǀƵƉŮ
 .ǀǆƁƾ ȿƆȮŽȚ
. Waliid: ahlan wa sahlan.
You are welcome.
.ǾɀƷ ȼŴȶ ǾƀȖ :ǋƸŽȶ

Grammar and Remarks
The Particle yyaa- ƾȿȮƁ
The particle yyaa has no meaning by itself. It serves as a stem to support an attached pro-
noun. In Iraqi Arabic there are verbs that may take two objects, the so-called direct and 
indirect objects in English. The direct object is usually in the form of an attached pronoun 
and the indirect object is a noun. But often the indirect object may be a pronoun also. 
Because two pronoun endings cannot be added to one verb in Arabic, the particle yyaa ƾȮȿƁ 
or iyyaa ƾȮȿƁȘ is used as a stem to take the attached pronoun of the direct object while the 
verb takes the attached pronoun of the indirect object. (Audio)
alla ykhalliilak il-ahal 
May God keep the family for you. 
ǚȼƀǈȚ ǙȼȮƴƸȿȮƴȼȮƈɀƁ ʇȚ
alla ykhalliilak yyaahum 
May God keep them for you. 
ǛȽƀƾƁ ǙȼȮƴƸȿȮƴȼȮƈɀƁ ʇȚ
alla ykhalliilak il-binit 
May God keep the girl for you. 
ǁȾȮƶȾȮƃɀȮŽȚ ǙȼȮƴƸȿȮƴȼȮƈɀƁ ʇȚ
alla ykhalliilak yyaaha 
May God keep her for you. 
ƾƀƾƁ ǙȼȮƴƸȿȮƴȼȮƈɀƁ ʇȚ
ajiiblich li-ktaab 
I bring you the book. 
țƾƄɀȮƳȮȾŽ ǶȾƴƃƸűȖ
ajiiblich iyyaa 
I bring it (him) to you. 
ȵƾȮȿƁȘ ǶȮȾƴɀƃƸűȖ
aktiblak risaala 
I write you a letter. 
ǀŽƾŴȾȤ ǙȼȮƴȮɀƃȮȾƄɀȮżȖ
aktiblak iyyaaha 
I write it (her) to you. 
ƾƀƾȮȿƁȘ ǙȼȮƴȮɀƃȾȮƄɀȮżȖ
yintiini ﬂuus 
He gives me money. 
ȦǞƴɀȮź ǠƶƸƭɀȮƶȾȮƁ
yintiini yyaaha 
He gives it (her) to me. 
ƾƀƾȿȮƁ ǠƶƸƭɀȮƶȾȮƁ
intiini s-sayyaara 
Give me the car! 
ȜȤƾȿƸȮ ȼŴ ǠƶƸƭɀȮſȘ
intiini yyaaha 
Give it (her) to me! 
ƾƀƾȮȿƁ ǠƶƸƭɀȮſȘ
The Feminine “t ǀȼȮ” Ending
As we discussed in lesson , nouns/things in Arabic are either masculine or feminine. There 
is no “it” as in English. Feminine nouns are usually marked by “a” in colloquial Arabic, the 
equivalent of the so-called feminine “t ǀȼȮ”(taa¢ marbuuta ǀŶǞŮɀǍȼž Ȕƾů) ending in classical 
Arabic. When a feminine word is followed by another noun in a possession construction, 
the ending -a is pronounced -at. When an attached pronoun or other suﬃx is added, the 
-at ending is written as “ȝ.”
 
Note: The feminine “t ǀȼȮ” is always preceded by the short vowel “a ȼȮ.” (Audio)

sayyaara
a car
ȜȼȤƾȿƸȮ ȼŴ
sayyaarat Kariim
Kariim’s car
ƗǍż ȜȼȤƾȼƸȮ ȼŴ
taalibat 
madrasa
school student
 ǀȼƃȮȾŽƾŶ
ǀ ȼŴȼȤɀǋȼž
The idaafa ǀȼȮźƾǤȘ Construction
The idaafa construction in Arabic is an arrangement when two or more nouns join together 
(without interruption) to form an equivalent in English to a phrase with “of, “ the posses-
sive “s” phrase, or two (or more) nouns used as attributives. (Audio)
taalib madrasa 
a school student (M) 
ǀ ȼŴȼȤɀǋȮȼž ƿŽƾŶ
taalibat madrasa 
a school student (F) 
ǀ ȼŴȼȤɀǋȼž ǀƃȮȾŽƾŶ
maktab Kaamil 
Kamil’s oﬃce 
ǚžƾż ƿƄɀȮƳȼž
maktabat Kaamil 
Kamil’s library 
ǚžƾż ǀƃƄɀȮƳȼž
sayyaarat mathaf 
a museum car 
ǗƇɀȮƄȼž ȜȼȤƾȿƸ ȼŴ
mufattish mataar 
an airport inspector 
Ȥƾƭž ǐƦȮƄȼȮƱȽž
muwazzaf maktab bank 
a bank oﬃce employee 
ǙƶȮȼŮ ƿƄɀȮƳȼž ǗȿȮŷȼǞȽž
muwazzafat maktab bank 
a bank oﬃce employee (F) 
ǙƶȮȼŮ ƿƄƳȼž ǀƱȿȮŷȼǞȽž
miftaah baab beet 
a house door key 
ǁƸŮ țƾŮ ȠƾƄɀȮƱȾž
kursi maktab chibiir 
a large oﬃce chair 
ǍƸȮƃȾȮǩ ƿƄɀȮƳȼž ǠŴɀǍȽȮż
sayyaarat mathaf chibiira 
a large museum car 
ȜǍƸȮƃȾȮǩ ǗƇɀȮƄȼž ȜȼȤƾȿƸ ȼŴ
 
In deﬁnite idaafa only the last noun in the chain takes the deﬁnite article “ il- ɀȮŽȘ,” 
which makes the whole phrase deﬁnite. Alternatively, the last noun can be a proper name, 
which is deﬁnite without il-. (Audio)
muwazzaf il-bank 
the employee of the bank 
ǙƶƃŽȚ ǗȿȮŷȼǞȮȽž
miftaah baab il-beet 
the house door key 
ǁƸƃŽȚ țƾŮ ȠƾƄɀȮƱȮȾž
taalibat jaamiat Baghdaad 
the Baghdad University student 
ȢȚǋưŮ ǀȼƯȮȾžƾű ǀȼƃȮȾŽƾŶ
kursi l-maktab ij-jibiir 
the large oﬃce chair 
ǍƸȮƃȮ ȿǰŽȚ ƿƄɀȮƳȼƓȚ ǠŴǍȽȮż
sayyaarat il-mathaf ij-jibiira 
the large museum car 
ȜǍƸȮƃȮ ȿǰŽȚ ǗƇƄƓȚ ȜȼȤƾȿƸȮ ȼŴ

 |  l e s s o n  
 
Note: Sometimes there are inner vowel variations depending on whether the added 
suﬃx is a vowel or begins in a vowel or consonant.
Idioms and Common Phrases (Audio)
1. maaku luzuum ȳȶǎȽȮŽ Ǟżƾž 
no need for (invariable)
maaku luzuum truuh 
lil-bariid
You (M) don’t need to go to 
the post oﬃce.
ǋƁǍȮȼƃɀȮƴȮȾŽ ȠȶǍɀȮů ȳȶǎȽȮŽ Ǟżƾž
maaku luzuum tshuufuun 
Laylaa
You (P) don’t need to see 
Laylaa.
ǟƴƸŽ ȴǞźǞƪɀȮů ȳȶǎȽȮŽ Ǟżƾž
maaku luzuum yjuun 
li-hnaa
They don’t need to come here.
ƾƶȮɀƷȮȾŽ ȴǞƆȮɀƁ ȳȶǎȽȮŽ Ǟżƾž
maaku luzuum adrus kull 
yoom
I don’t need to study every 
day.
ȳǞƁ ǚȽȮż ȦȽȤɀȢȖ ȳȶǎȽȮŽ Ǟżƾž
maaku luzuum tzuuriin 
it-tabiib
You (F) don’t need to visit 
the doctor.
ƿƸƃȿȮƭŽȚ ǜƁȤȶǎɀȮů ȳȶǎȽȮŽ Ǟżƾž
maaku luzuum tijiin 
wiyyaahum
You (F) don’t need to come 
with them.
ǛƀƾȮȿƁȾȶ ƞƆȮȾů ȳȶǎȽȮŽ Ǟżƾž
2. bala adab țȼȢȖ ǾȮȼŮ

He is impolite.
țȼȢȖ ǾŮ Ǟƀ
humma bala adab
They are impolite.
țȼȢȖ ǾŮ ǝȿƵȮȽƀ
Kariima taakul bala adab
Kariima eats with bad 
manners.
țȼȢȖ ǾŮ ǚȽȮżƾů ǀƚǍż
il-wilid yilibuun bala 
adab
The boys play rudely.
țȼȢȖ ǾŮ ȴǞƃɀƯȾȮƴȾȮƁ ǋȾȮŽȾǞɀȮŽȘ
huwwa yimshi b-nuss 
ish-shaari bala adab
He walks rudely in the middle 
of the street.
 ȬȤƾȿȮƪŽȚ ȿǑȽȮſ ɀț ǠƪɀƵȾȮƁ Ǟƀ
țȼȢȖ ǾŮ

triid

 ȆǝȼȮſƾƳȼž ɀț Ǡ ȾƪɀȮƴȽȮż
ǋƁǍɀȮů ȼȳ ǚȮȾƅȮȾž
rah-akhalli kullshi b-makaana

 ɀț Ǡ ȾƪɀȮƴȽȮż ǠƦȮƴȼȮųȖ ɀȠȼȤ
ǝȼȮſƾƳȼž
la-tiqlaq kullshi b-makaana

all right.
 ɀț Ǡ ȾƪɀȮƴȽȮż ǘƴɀȮƲȮȾƄȼȮŽ
ǝȼȮſƾƳȼž

6. Replace the words in parentheses with the right form of yyaa ƾȮȿƁ :
Example: jiibli (il-binit) mnil-madrasa 
ǀ ȼŴȼȤɀǋȼƵɀȮŽȚ ǜɀž (ǁȾȮƶȾȮƃŽȚ) ǠƴȮɀƃƸű
 
jiibli yyaaha mnil-madrasa 
ǀ ȼŴȼȤɀǋȼƵɀȮŽȚ ǜɀž ƾƀƾȮȿƁ ǠƴȮɀƃƸű
siddli (ish-shubbaach), min fadlak  
 ǙȮƴƬź ǜȾž Ȇ(ɃƾȿƃǇȮƪŽȚ) ǠŽɀǋȮ ȾŴ
dalliini (il-bariid), alla ykhalliik  
 ǙƸȿȮƴȼȮƈȮɀƁ ʇȚ Ȇ(ǋƁǍƃŽȚ) ǠƶƸȿȮŽȼȢ
alla ykhalliilkum (il-wilid wil-banaat)  
 ȝƾƶƃŽȚȶ ǋŽǞŽȚ) ǛƳɀȮƴƸȼȮƴȼȮƈȾƁ ʇȚ)
sallihli (is-sayyaara), min fadlak  
 ǙƴƬź ǜȾž Ȇ(ȜȤƾƸ ȿƉŽȚ) Ǡƴ ɀƇƦȮƴ ȼǧ

 ǍȾȮǩƾŮ (ȝƾŸƾ ȿƉŽȚ) ǠƴƸ ȾƇƦȮƴ ȼǧ

 ǠŮǍƯŽƾŮ (ǀŽƾŴƦǍȮŽȚ) ǠŽȚǍɀȮŻȘ

 ǁƸƃŽƾŮ (ǀƭɀȮƶȮȿƐȚ) ǠƴƸȿȮƴȼȮų
7. 
Translate the following into Arabic:
May God keep them for you.
 
May God keep the children for you (F).
 
Let me bring you the car.
 
I am bringing your (M) car.
 
My nephew and my sister live in Basra.

Some Additional Body Parts: (Audio)
Note that any part of the body which consists of two (e.g. two eyes, two hands, etc.) is 
generally feminine in Arabic.

Grammar and Remarks
1. Ordinal Numbers First to Tenth:
The ordinal numbers in Iraqi Arabic are of two genders, masculine and feminine. Except 
for “ﬁrst” the ordinal numbers have consistent forms. There are no ordinal numbers higher 
than tenth; the cardinal numbers are used instead. (Audio)
Numeral 
Masculine 
Feminine

Ordinal numbers may come either after or before nouns. After the noun they are regu-
lar adjectives and must agree with the noun in gender and in deﬁniteness. Before the noun 
they must always be masculine singular and the noun indeﬁnite although such phrases are 
deﬁnite in meaning. (Audio)

2. Cardinal Numbers Higher than 100
The words for “hundred, thousand, and million” are nouns and have three number forms:
singular, dual, and plural (see lessons –). (Audio)

 |  l e s s o n  

thousands

malaayiin

3. Counting: hsaab țƾƉȮɀŲ
The Arabic system of counting (objects/nouns) requires attention from the student 
because of its number forms (S/D/P). Below are counting tables for two nouns (M and F). 
(Audio)

ǋȮȾŽȾȶ ǂȼȮƴɀȮůȶ ǀǆƸȮȾž
 
  
 
miyya w tlath saaaat (to ) ȝƾŸƾŴ ǂȼȮƴɀȮůȶ ǀǆƸȮȾž

Medical Care |  
Notes on Counting:
 . One (ǋȾŲȚȶ) and two (ƞȮƶɀȮŰ) have exceptional forms, which are diﬀerent from the more 
consistent numbers three to ten.
 . To say “one boy,” only the word for boy needs to be uttered, although the number one 
“waahid ǋȾŲȚȶ” or “wihda ȜǋɀŲȾȶ ,” depending on the gender of the word, can be added 
after the noun.
 . Counting two of one kind, there is a special dual form in Arabic, which is made by 
adding the suﬃx “-een or -teen ƞů ȆǜƁ” to the singular noun, masculine and feminine, 
respectively (see Lesson ).
 . The counted noun is plural only from three to ten. Then from eleven and higher the noun 
must be singular again.
 . When counting from three to ten, the vowel ending “a” of the independent number must 
be omitted (see above). There are also other dropping, shifting and assimilation.
 . The independent number one hundred “miyya ǀǆƸȮȾž” becomes “miit ǁƸȾž” in counting.
Idioms and Common Phrases (Audio)
1. sh-bii + attached pronoun? 
+ ȒǠȾȮƃɀȮŵ 
What’s wrong with (him)? (lit. What’s in him?)
sh-bii Salmaan?
What’s wrong with Salman?
ȒȴƾƵɀȮƴ ȼŴ ǝƸȾȮƃȮ ɀŵ
sh-biiha Samiira il-yoom?
What’s wrong with Samiira today?
ȒȳǞƸŽȚ ȜǍƸƵ ȼŴ ƾƷƸȾȮƃȮ ɀŵ
sh-biihum it-tullaab?
What’s wrong with the students?
ȒțǾǇȮƭŽȚ ǛƷƸȾȮƃȮ ɀŵ
sh-biik? leesh inta 
bil-mustashfa?
What’s wrong with you (M)? Why 
are you in the hospital?
 ǁɀȮſȘ ǐƸŽ ȒǙƸȾȮƃȮ ɀŵ
ȒǟƱɀȮƪȼȮƄȮ ɀƉȽƵɀȮŽƾŮ
2. 9afya 9alee + attached pronoun 
+ ǠȮƴȮȼŸ ǝȼȮƸɀȮƱȮȼŸ 
Good for (him), bravo
afya aleek, tih chi arabi 
zeen
Good for you (M), you speak 
good Arabic.
 Ǡǰ ɀƇȮȾů ȆǙƸȮƴȮȼŸ ǝȼȮƸɀȮƱȮȼŸ
ǜƁȥ ǠŮǍŸ
afya aleech, tudursiin 
bij-jaamia
Good for you (F), you are 
studying at the university.
 ƞŴɀȤȽǋȽȮů ȆǶƸȮƴȮȼŸ ǝȼȮƸɀȮƱȮȼŸ
ǀƯȾžƾȮȿƐƾŮ
afya alee, sihhta kullish 
zeena
Good for him, his health is 
very good.
 ǝȼȮƄȮ ɀƇ Ⱦǧ ȆǝƸȮƴȮȼŸ ǝȼȮƸɀȮƱȮȼŸ
ǀƶƁȥ ǐƦȮƴȽȮż
afya aleeha, faazat 
bis-sibaaq

 ȝȼȥƾź ȆƾƷƸȮƴȮȼŸ ǝȼȮƸɀȮƱȮȼŸ
ȰƾƃȮ ƦƉŽƾŮ
3. balkat, balki ǠƳɀȮƴȮȼŮ Ȇ ɀǁȼȮƳɀȮƴȼŮ 
Perhaps, may (invariable)
balkat yiji baad shwayya
Perhaps he will come shortly.
ǀǆȮƁȼǞɀȮŵ ǋȮȼƯȮȼŮ ǠƆȾȮƁ ɀǁȼȮƳɀȮƴȼŮ
balki yruuhuun ydursuun 
b-Baghdaad
They may go to study in 
Baghdad.
 ɀț ȴǞŴɀȤȽǋȮɀƁ ȴǞŲȶǍȮɀƁ ǠȾȮƳɀȮƴȼŮ
ȢȚǋưŮ
laazim ashuufa hissa, 
balkat ysaaﬁr il-yoom

travel today.
 ɀǁȼȮƳɀȮƴȼŮ Ȇǝ ȿƉȮȾƀ ǝźǞŵȖ ȳȾȥǽ
ȳǞƸŽȚ ǍȾźƾƉɀƁ

bil-iraaq

.ǀſƾƃɀƯȼȮůȶ ȦȚȤ ǕȾȮűȾȶ ƾƀɀǋȮȾŸ ȴƾǩ ǀƵƉŮ

Grammar and Remarks
Comparative and Superlative:
The comparatives and the superlatives such as “taller, tallest,” are predictable in Iraqi Arabic. 
They are usually derived from adjectives of associated meanings. They are invariable in form 
with no gender or number. The diﬀerence between the comparative and the superlative 
adjectives is in the structuring of the sentence. (Audio)

Media: Radio, Television, and Journalism |  

1. Comparative: (Audio)
sayyaarti arkhas 
My car is cheaper. 
ǑȼȮųɀȤȖ ǠůȤƾȿƸȮ ȼŴ
barnaamij atwal 
a longer program 
ȲȼǞɀȮŶȖ ǃȾžƾȮſɀǍȮȼŮ
Baabil aqdam 
Babylon is older. 
ȳȼǋɀȮŻȖ ǚŮƾŮ
 
When two objects are compared, the preposition “min ǜȮȾž” is used, which is the equiv-
alent of “than” in English in such constructions.
Laylaa akbar min Basma
Laylaa is older than Basma
ǀƵƉŮ ǜȾž ǍȼȮƃɀȮżȖ ǟƴƸŽ
beetna asghar min beetkum
Our house is smaller than your 
house.
ǛƳƄƸŮ ǜȾž ǍȼȮư ɀǧȖ ƾƶȮƄƸŮ
Baabil aqdam min Baghdaad
Babylon is older than Baghdad.
ȢȚǋưŮ ǜȾž ȳȼǋɀȮŻȖ ǚŮƾŮ
2. Superlative:
The superlative is usually constructed either with the comparative taking the deﬁnite arti-
cle “il- ɀȮŽȘ, the,” or with the following noun as a deﬁnite plural, or with a pronoun suﬃx 
attached to the comparative, depending on the context. (Audio)
Laylaa il-atwal 
Laylaa is the tallest. 
ȲȼǞɀȮŶǈȚ ǟƴƸŽ
Laylaa atwal il-banaat 
Laylaa is the tallest girl. 
ȝƾƶȮȼƃɀȮŽȚ ȲȼǞɀȮŶȖ ǟƴƸŽ
Laylaa atwalhum 
Laylaa is the tallest. 
ǛȽƷɀȮŽȼǞɀȮŶȖ ǟƴƸŽ
Baabil il-aqdam 
Babylon is the oldest. 
ȳȼǋȮɀȮŻǈȚ ǚŮƾŮ
Baabil aqdam il-mudun 
Babylon is the oldest city. 
ȴȽǋȮȽƵɀȮŽȚ ȳȼǋȮɀȮŻȖ ǚŮƾŮ
Baabil agdamhum 
Babylon is the oldest. 
ǛȽƷɀžȼǋȮɀȮŻȖ ǚŮƾŮ

 |  l e s s o n  
 
There are certain adjectives such as “mit¢assif Ǘ ƦŴƺɀȮƄȾž (to be sorry), farhaan ȴƾŲɀǍȼȮź 
(happy), and juuaan ȴƾŸǞű (hungry),” that do not have associated comparative forms. In 
such cases the regular adjective is used with the comparative words “akthar ǍȼȮƅɀȮżȖ (more),” 
or “aqal ǚȼȮŻȖ (less)” as required. (Audio)
aani mit¢assif akthar minnak
I am more sorry than you.
ǙǆȮƶȾž ǍȼȮƅɀȮżȖ Ǘ ƦŴƺɀȮƄȾž Ǡſȕ
hiyya farhaana akthar minhum
She is happier than they.
ǛƷɀȮƶȾž ǍƅɀȮżȖ ǀſƾŲɀǍȼȮź Ǡƀ
intu juaaniin aqal min Basma
You are less hungry than 
Basma.
 ǜȾž ǚȼȮŻȖ ƞſƾŸǞű ǞƄſȘ
ǀƵƉŮ

Idioms and Common Phrases (Audio)
1. 9ala jayya ǀȮǆƸȮȼű ǟƴŸ 
On the way, coming, arriving soon (invariable)
Q: ween Samiir?
Where is Samir?
ȒǍƸƵŴ ǜƁȶ
A: huwwa bil-idhaaa, bass 
ala jayya
He is in the radio station, 
but arriving soon.
ǀȮǆƸȮȼű ǟƴŸ ǏȼŮ ȆǀȼŸȚȣȁƾŮ Ǟƀ
il-muraasil ala jayya 
mnil-maktab

the oﬃce.
 ǜȮɀž ǀȮǆƸȮȼű ǟƴŸ ǚȮ ȾŴȚǍȽƵɀȮŽȘ
ƿȼȮƄɀȮƳȼƵɀȮŽȚ
huwwa muu hnaa, bass ala 
jayya
His is not here, but 
arriving soon.
ǀȮǆƸȮȼű ǟƴŸ ǏŮ ȆƾƶȮɀƀ Ǟž Ǟƀ
ihna ala jayya mnil-mataar

airport.
 ǜȮɀž ǝȮǆƸȮȼű ǟƴŸ ƾƶȮɀŲȘ
ȤƾƭȼƵɀȮŽȚ
2. maaku chaara ȜȼȤƾȮǩ ǞȽȮżƾž 
No cure, hopeless, no way out (invariable)
maaku chaara, laazim 
asaaﬁr lil-iraaq
No way out, I must travel 
to Iraq.
 ǍȮȾźƾŴȖ ȳȾȥǽ ȆȜȼȤƾȮǩ Ǟżƾž
ȰȚǍƯɀȮƴȮȾŽ
maaku chaara, huwwa 
kullish mariid
There is no hope, he is 
very sick.
ǒƁǍȼž ǐȮƦȮƴȽȮż Ǟƀ ȆȜȼȤƾȮǩ Ǟżƾž

is-sarataan

disease of cancer.
 ȩȼǍȮȼž ɀȲȘ ȆȜȼȤƾȮǩ Ǟżƾž

muraasil ajnabi
Joon Dalas muraasil ajnabi min majallat il-Taaymz il-Amriikiyya. huwwa b-Baghdaad yhibb 
yiktib taqriir sahaﬁ an il-hadaara il-iraaqiyya il-qadiima. huwwa yriid ysawwi muqaabla 
wiyya muwazzaf ib-wizaarat il-Ilaam ib-Baghdaad. hassal muqaabla maa sayyid Jamaal 
mas¢uul qisim il-ajaanib bil-wizaara. il-muqaabala rah tkuun yoom il-arbaaa is-saaa 
tisa sabahan. il-muraasil Joon wil-muwazzaf Jamaal tqablaw ib-maktab Jamaal. awwal

Telephone Conversations
mukhaabaraat 
 ȝȚǍȼȮŮƾƈȮȽž
Dialing telephone numbers in Iraq or in any other Arab country is similar to dialing in the 
western countries; numbers are read from left to right. Basma is making a telephone call to 
the home of a relative in Baghdad, and Kariim, her cousin, answers the phone.
Basic Dialogue (Audio)
. Kariim: aloo, aloo. minu yitkallam?
Hello, hello. Who is speaking?
ȒǛǆȮƴȼȮƳɀȮƄȾȮƁ ǞƶȮȾž .ǞŽȖ ȆǞŽȖ :ƗǍż
. Basma: masaa¢ il-kheer . . . aloo . . .
Good afternoon . . . hello . . .
. . . ǞŽȖ . . . ǍƸƒȚ ȔƾƉž :ǀƵƉŮ
. Kariim: ma-da-asma zeen. il-khatt 
mshawwash.
I can’t hear you well. There is static on the 
line.
.ȧǆǞȼȮƪȮɀž ǓȼȮƒȘ .ǜƁȥ ǕȼƵ ɀŴȖ ȼȢ ȼȳ :ƗǍż
. Basma: hissa, tismani ahsan?
Now, do you hear me better?
ȒǜȮ ȼƉɀŲȖ ǠƶɀƯȮȼƵ ɀƉȾů Ȇǝ ǆƉȮȾƀ :ǀƵƉŮ
. Kariim: hwaaya ahsan. minu hadirtich?
Much better. Who are you?
ȒǶȮȾůɀǍ ȾƬȼŲ ǞƶȮȾž .ǜ ȼƉɀŲȖ ǝȮȼƁȚǞɀƀ :ƗǍż
. Basma: aani Basma min Los Angeles.
I am Basma from Los Angeles.
.ȦǞƴƍȚ ȦǞŽ ǜȾž ǀƵƉŮ Ǡſȕ :ǀƵƉŮ

 |  l e s s o n  
. Kariim: Basma! ahlan w sahlan. 
il-hamdilla as-salaama.
Basma! Welcome. Praise be to God for your 
safe arrival.
 ȼȬ ʇȾǋȮɀƵȼƑȚ .ȹǾƷŴȶ ȹǾƀȖ !ǀƵƉŮ :ƗǍż
.ǀžǾȮ ǆƉŽȚ
. Basma: shukran jaziilan. shloonkum? 
inshaalla zeeniin?

God willing?
 ȒǛƳɀȮſǞƴɀȮŵ .ȹǾƁǎű ȹȚǍƳŵ :ǀƵƉŮ
ȒǜȮƸƶȮƁȥ ʇƾƪɀȮſȘ
. Kariim: ihna zeeniin il-hamdu lillaah. 
aash min sima sootich.
We are well, thank God. Nice to hear your 
voice.
 ǜȮȾž ȧƾŸ Ȇʇ ȽǋƵ ȼƇɀȮŽȚ ǜȮƸƶȮƁȥ ƾƶɀŲȘ :ƗǍż
ȾǶȮȾůǞ Ƚǧ ǕȼƵȮ ȾŴ
. Basma: aani wsalit gabul yoomeen. 
haawalit akhaaburkum il-baarha, bass 
il-khatt chaan mashghuul.
I arrived two days ago. I tried to call you 
yesterday but the line was busy.
 .ƞžǞƁ ǚȮȽƃȼȮż ǁȮȾƴ ȼǧɀȶ Ǡſȕ :ǀƵƉŮ
 ȴƾǩ ǓȼȮƒȚ ǏȼŮ ȆǀŲɀȤƾƃŽȚ ǛȽżɀǍȽŮƾųȖ ǁȾŽȼȶƾŲ
ȲǞưɀȮƪȼž
. Kariim: adri. binti Laylaa tih chi 
hwaaya at-talifoon.
I know. My daughter Laylaa is always 
talking on the telephone.
 ȼȬ ǝȼƁȚǞȮɀƀ Ǡǰ ɀƇȮȾů ǟƴƸŽ ǠƄɀȮƶȾȮŮ .ȸȤɀȢȖ :ƗǍż
ȴǞƱȾȮƴǆȮƄȮŽȚ
. Basma: aani mushtaaqatilkum w ahibb 
azuurkum.

 ƿȮȾŲȖȶ ǛȽȮƳɀȮƴȮȾƄȼȮŻƾȮƄɀȮƪȽž Ǡſȕ :ǀƵƉŮ
ǛżɀȤȶȥȖ

ween naazla?
We miss you more. Where are you staying?
 ǜƁȶ ǠƄſȘ .ǍȼȮƅɀȮżȖ ƞȮŻƾƄɀȮƪȽž ƾƶɀŲȘ :ƗǍż
ȒǀȼȮŽɀȥƾſ

ghurfa raqam .
I am staying at the Rashid Hotel, room

 ȆǋƸȮŵǆǍȮŽȚ ȰȾǋɀȮƶȮȾź ɀț ǀȼȮŽɀȥƾſ Ǡſȕ :ǀƵƉŮ
 ǛȼȮŻȼȤ ǀźɀǍȽȮŹ

. Basma: raqam il-ﬁndiq: -
The hotel number is -.
- :ȰȾǋɀȮƶȮȾƱɀȮŽȚ ǛȼȮŻȼȤ :ǀƵƉŮ
. Kariim: aani hissa jaayy bis-sayyaara 
lil-ﬁndiq

 ȜȤƾƸȮ ǆƉŽƾŮ ȸƾű ǝ ǆƉȮȾƀ Ǡſȕ :ƗǍż

Grammar and Remarks
Conditional Sentences (if):
Iraqi Arabic has two words used for the conditional sentences “if.” The word “ idhaa ȚȣȘ, if ” 
is used to express real and possible conditions (If you come we will go together.) and the 
word “loo ǞŽ, if ” for unreal and impossible conditions (If you had come we would have gone 
together.).
1. Real Condition with idhaa ȚȣȘ 
if
A sentence for a real and possible condition is expressed with the word “idhaa ȚȣȘ , if,” fol-
lowed usually by a perfect (past) verb. However an imperfect (present) verb may also be used. 
Sentences with perfect or imperfect verbs have the same meaning in English. The verb in the 
second part of the sentence (the result clause) may be imperative or imperfect verb. (Audio)
idhaa rihit l-Baghdaad, 
shuuf Baabil
If you go (lit. went) to 
Baghdad, see Babylon.
ǚŮƾŮ ȯǞŵ ȢȚǋưŮ ɀȲ ǁȾŲȾȤ ȚȣȘ
idhaa truuh l-Baghdaad, 
shuuf Baabil.
If you go to Baghdad, see 
Babylon.
ǚŮƾŮ ȯǞŵ ȢȚǋưŮ ɀȲ ȠȶǍɀȮů ȚȣȘ
idhaa ykhaaburni asi¢la
If he calls me, I will ask him.
ǝȼȮƴɀȮƂȮ ȾŴȖ ǠſɀǍȮȽŮƾƈɀƁ ȚȣȘ
idhaa khaabarni asi¢la
If he calls (lit. called) me, I 
will ask him.
ǝȼȮƴɀȮƂȮ ȾŴȖ ǠſɀǍȮȼŮƾų ȚȣȘ
2. Unreal Condition with loo ǞŽ 
if
The word “loo ǞŽ, if,” is used for unreal and impossible conditions (contrary to the fact) fol-
lowed usually by a participle or a perfect tense verb, although an imperfect verb may also be 
used. The verb in the second part of the sentence (the result clause) consists of “chaan ȴƾǩ” 
(invariable) followed by a perfect or imperfect verb conjugated to agree with the subject. 
(Audio)
loo raayhiin lil-maktab, 
chaan shaafaw il-mudiir
If they had gone to the oﬃce, 
they would have seen the 
director.
 ȴƾǩ ȆƿȼȮƄɀȮƳȼƵɀȮƴȮȾŽ ƞƇɀƁȚȤ ǞŽ
ǍƁǋȮȽƓȚ ɀǞȼȮźƾŵ
loo zaayir il-iraaq, chaan 
shaaf nahar Dijla
If he had visited Iraq, he 
would have seen the Tigris 
River.
 ȯƾŵ ȴƾǩ ȆȰȚǍƯŽȚ ǍȾȮƁȚȥ ǞŽ
ǀȮƴȮɀűȾȢ ǍȼƷȼȮſ
loo jiit, chaan rihna siwiyya
If you had come we would 
have gone together.
ǝȮǆƁȾǞȮ ȾŴ ƾƶȮɀŲȾȤ ȴƾǩ ȆǁƸű ǞŽ
loo yuruf qraaya, chaan 
diras arabi
If he knew reading, he would 
have studied Arabic.
 ȦȼȤȾȢ ȴƾǩ ȆǀȮȼƁȚǍɀȮŻ ȯȽǍȮɀƯȽƁ ǞŽ
ǠŮǍŸ
loo indi waqit, chaan rihit 
wiyyaak
If I had the time, I would 
have gone with you.
 ǁȮȾŲȾȤ ȴƾǩ ȆǁȮȾŻȼȶ ȸǋɀȮƶȾŸ ǞŽ
ȱƾȮȿƁȾȶ

Telephone Conversations |  
The Relative Pronoun (i)lli ǠƦȮŽȘ 
Who(m), whose, that, which
The English relative pronouns “who, whom, whose, that, and which,” whether they are 
referring back to people or things, masculine, feminine, singular, or plural, are expressed in 
Iraqi Arabic by the word “(i)lli ǠƦȮŽȘ.” However, “(i)lli” is used only when the noun in the 
principal sentence is deﬁnite. There is no need for it if the noun is indeﬁnite. “(i)lli” and 
the related word “min ǜȾž, who” are also used in the beginning of phrases that correspond 
in English to “He who, whoever, anyone who, whatever, that which,” sentences that usually 
have proverbial meanings. (Audio)
il-ustaadh illi ydarris 
arabi
the professor who teaches 
Arabic

Idioms and Common Phrases (Audio)
1. 9aash min sima9 soot + attached pronoun 
+ ȝǞ Ƚǧ ǕȼƵ ȾŴ ǜȮȾž ȧƾŸ 
Nice to hear 
your voice. (lit. He who hears [your] voice will live. A compliment one says to another after an 
absence, see also lesson 8).
haay weenak? aash min 
sima sootak.
Where have you been? Nice 
to hear your voice.

ween hal-gheeba?

aani mushtaaqlak. aash 
min sima sootak

2. minu abu baachir? ȒǍȾȮǩƾŮ ǞŮȖ ǞƶȮȾž 
Who knows about tomorrow? Life is too short. 
(lit. Who is the father of tomorrow? Encourages one to enjoy life.)
ishtireet sayyaara jidiida. 
minu abu baachir?
I bought a new car. Life is 
too short.
 ȜȤƾȿƸȮ ȼŴ ǁƁǍȮȾƄɀȮŵȘ
ȒǍȾȮǩƾŮ ǞŮȖ ǞƶȮȾž .ȜǋƁǋȾȮű
saafar il-Awrubba yitwannas. 
minu abu baachir?
He went to Europe for fun. 
Life is too short.
 .ǏǆȮſȼǞɀȮƄȾȮƁ ƾȮȿŮȼȤɀȶȖ ɀȲ ǍȼȮźƾŴ
ȒǍȾȮǩƾŮ ǞŮȖ ǞƶȮȾž
minu abu baachir? khalli 
nshuuf id-dinya
Who knows about 
tomorrow? Let us enjoy life.
 ǠƦȮƴȼȮų ȒǍȾȮǩƾŮ ǞŮȖ ǞƶȮȾž
ƾƸɀȮſƦǋȮŽȚ ȯǞƪɀȮſ
leesh inta ussi? minu abu 
baachir?

knows about tomorrow?

1. Give appropriate replies orally to the following:

minu raah lil-ﬁndiq bis-sayyaara?

DARIS ISHRIIN  ǜȮƁǍɀȮƪȾŸ ȦȾȤȼȢ
Cultural and Folkloric Tales
qusas hadaariyya wa shabiyya 
 ǀȮǆƸȾȮƃȮɀƯȼȮŵȶ ǀǆȮƁȤƾƬȼŲ ɀǑ ȼƫȽȮŻ
Modern Iraq was ancient Mesopotamia, the land between the two rivers, the Tigris and 
the Euphrates. Most historians call ancient Iraq the cradle of civilization. Its archaeo-
logical remains date as far back as the Paleolithic Age, , years ago. Mesopotamia 
contributed many inventions and innovations to civilization: the ﬁrst writing, school, law, 
map, math, irrigation, wheel, chariot, contract and court, the ﬁrst kingdom and empire, 
metallurgy, astronomy, and literature. The “Epic of Gilgamesh,” about ﬁve thousand years 
old, is a magniﬁcent literary work that preceded Homer’s Iliad and Odyssey by more than 
two thousand years. Ancient Mesopotamians were sophisticated builders who built great 
monuments and cities, among them Ur, Uruk, Nineveh, and Babylon. Babylon was the 
city where the “Hanging Gardens,” one of the world’s Seven Wonders, once stood. After a 
few centuries of foreign occupation, Iraq ﬂourished again in the th century, when Bagh-
dad became the capital of the Islamic empire. Baghdad’s scientists, scholars, philosophers, 
mathematicians, educators, physicians, writers, translators, poets, singers, musicians, artists, 
traders, and artisans are all to be found in the tales of A Thousand and One Nights, which 
speaks of the glorious city that was Baghdad. One can imagine that with such a long history 
Iraq must have had very rich traditions of legends and tales of fabled cities, caliphs, kings, 
princes, wise men, jinns, and animals.
 
Basma is strolling in the book market “Suug is-Saraay,” one of oldest markets in Bagh-
dad. She stops by a bookstore inquiring about some old books about Baghdad, when the 
owner of the shop begins to intrigue her with folk tales on the literary history of the city.

 |  l e s s o n  
1. The Story of the Caliph Harun ir-Rashid and Abu Nuwas
The Caliph Harun ir-Rashid, whose reign is considered to be the golden age of the Islamic 
empire, ruled from  to . Abu Nuwas was a famous poet whose life was full of 
intrigues.
Title: “il-9udhur aqbah mnidh-dhanib” (Audio)
yoom mnil-ayyaam il-Khaliifa Haruun ir-Rashiid chaan da-yitmashsha b-hadiiqat qasra.
w shaaf Abu Nuwaas da-yishrab nabiidh bil-hadiiqa.
il-Khaliifa ghidab hwaaya li¢an il-Khalifa huwwa Amiir il-Mu¢miniin.
lamman Abu Nuwaas shaaf il-Khaliifa khaaf w damm butil in-nabiidh waraa.
il-Khaliifa si¢al abu Nuwaas “haay shunu daamm wara zahrak?”
Abu Nuwaas gaal “ butil mayy, mawlaay.”
il-Khaliifa galla “khalliini ashuufa.”
Abu Nuwaas shawwaf il-butil lil-Khaliifa.
il-Khaliifa shaaf il-butil bii nabiidh ahmar, w galla “shloon il-mayy ykuun ahmar?”
Abu Nuwaas galla “mawlaay, il-mayy min shaafak khijal minnak w saar ahmar.”
il-Khaliifa kullish ghidab ala Abu Nuwaas w haddada bil-qatil.
bass Abu Nuwas twassal bil-Khaliifa hatta yiﬁ anna.
il-Khaliifa galla “aﬁ annak bi-sharit waahid, huwwa tintiini udhur aqbah 
mnidh-dhanib.”
Abu Nuwaas galla “mawlaay, shloon agdar antiik udhur aqbah mnidh-dhanb?”
il-Khaliifa galla “maa aruf. loo tintiini udhur aqbah mnidh-dhanib loo aqutlak.” w raah.
baad kam yoom, Abu Nuwaas shaaf il-Khaliﬀa da-yitmashsha bil-hadiiqa. raah wara 
il-Khaliifa w girasa min tiiza.
il-Khaliifa gumaz wi-tajjab. minu yigdar yigrus Amiir il-Mu¢miniin b-qasra?
il-Khaliifa indaar da-yshuuf minu girasa, shaaf abu Nuwaas. galla “haay shloon tijjarra¢ 
tugrus Amiir il-Mu¢miniin?!”
Abu Nuwaas galla “il-afu mawlaay, aani fakkarit inta Zubayda.”
il-Khaliifa ghidab akthar li¢ann Zubayda hiyya zawijta. w galla “shloon tijjarra¢ tugrus 
zawijti Zubayda, Umm il-Mu¢miniin?!”
Abu Nuwaas galla “mawlaay, aani maa ajjara¢ agrus Zubayda, bass haadha udhri. inta 
tidhdhakkar tilabit minni antiik udhur aqbah mnidh-dhanib?”
il-Khaliifa Harun ir-Rashiid dihak hwaaya, w ifa an Abu Nuwaas.
“ƿȾȮſǆ ǌȮŽȚ ǜɀž ǉȼƃɀȮŻȖ ȤǌȮȽƯɀȮŽȘ” :ȴȚǞɀȮƶȮȽŸ
.ȵȼǍ ɀƫȼȮŻ ǀȼȮƲƁǋȼŲ ɀț ƾǆȮƪȼƵɀȮƄȾȮƁȼ Ȣ ȴƾǩ ǋƸȮŵǆǍȮŽȚ ȴȶȤƾƀ ǀƱƸƴƒȘ ȆȳƾƁǈȚ ǜȮɀž ȳǞƁ
.ǀƲƁǋȮȼƑƾŮ ǌƸȮƃȼȮſ țȼǍɀȮƪȾȮƁ ȼȢ ȦȚǞȽȮſ ǞŮȚ ȯƾŵȶ
.ƞƶȾžƻȮȽƓȚ ǍƸžȖ Ǟƀ ǀƱƸƴƒȚ ȴǈ ǝȼƁȚǞȮɀƀ ƿȼȮƬȮȾŹ ǀƱƸƴƒȘ
.ȵȚȤȼȶ ǌƸȮƃǆȮƶȮŽȚ ǚ ȾƭȮȽŮ ȿǛ ȼǤȶ ȯƾų ǀƱƸƴƒȚ ȯƾŵ ȦȚǞſ ǞŮȚ ǜȮǆƵȼȮŽ

Cultural and Folkloric Tales |  
“ȒȱȼǍɀƷȼȮŷ ȚȼȤȼȶ ȳƾǤ ǞƶȽȮŵ ȸƾƀ” ȦȚǞſ ǞŮȚ ǚȿȮƂȮ ȾŴ ǀƱƸƴƒȘ
“.ȸǽɀǞȮȼž ȆɀǠȼž ǚ ȾƭȽŮ” ȲƾǬ ȦȚǞſ ǞŮȚ
“.ǝȼȮźǞȽȮŵȖ ǠƶȮƸƦȮƴȼȮų” ǝȼȮƴɀȮƴȼȮǬ ǀƱƸƴƒȘ
.ǀƱƸƴƈɀȮƴȮȾŽ ǚ ȾƭȽƃŽȚ ȯǆǞȼȮŵ ȦȚǞſ ǞŮȚ
“ȒǍȼƵɀŲȖ ȴǞƳȮɀƁ ɀǠȼƵɀȮŽȚ ȴǞƴɀȮŵ” ǝȼȮƴɀȮƴȼȮǬ ȶ ȆǍȼƵȮɀŲȖ ǌƸƃȼȮſ ǠŮ ǚ ȾƭȽƃŽȚ ȯƾŵ ǀƱƸƴƒȘ
“.ǍȼƵɀŲȖ Ȥƾǧȶ ǙǆȮƶȮȾž ǚȮ ȼƆȮȾų ǙȼȮźƾŵ ǜȮȾž ɀǠȮȼƓȚ ȆȸǽɀǞȮȼž” ǝȼȮƴɀȮƴȼȮǬ ȦȚǞſ ǞŮȚ
.ǚȮȾƄȼȮƲɀȮŽƾȾȮŮ ȵȼȢǆǋȮȼƀȶ ȦȚǞſ ǞŮȚ ǟƴȼŸ ƿ ȼƬȾŹ ǐȮƦȮƴȽȮǬ ǀƱƸƴƈȮŽȘ
.ǝǆȮƶȮȼŸ ǠƱȮɀƯȾȮƁ ǟȿȮƄŲ ǀƱƸƴƒƾȾȮŮ ǚȮ ǆŴȼǞɀȮů ȦȚǞſ ǞŮȚ ǏȮȼŮ
.“ƿȾȮſǆ ǌȮŽȚ ǜȮɀž ǉȮȼƃɀȮŻȖ ȤȽ ǌȮȽŸ ǠƶȮƸƭɀȮƶȮȾů Ǟƀ ȆǋȮȾŲȚȶ ȪɀǍȼȮƪȾȮŮ ǙǆȮƶȮȼŸ ǠƱȮɀŸȖ” ǝȼȮƴɀȮƴȼȮǬ ǀƱƸƴƒȘ
“ȒƿɀȮſǆ ǌȮŽȚ ǜȮɀž ǉȼƃɀȮŻȖ ȤȽ ǌȮȽŸ ǙƸƭɀȮſȖ ȤȼǋɀȮǬ Ȗ ȴǞƴȮ ɀŵ ȆȸǽɀǞȮȼž” ǝȼȮƴɀȮƴȼȮǬ ȦȚǞſ ǞŮȚ
.ȠȚȤȶ .“ǙȼȮƴɀȮƄȽȮŻȖ ǞŽ ƿȾȮſǆ  ǌȮŽȚ ǜȮɀž ǉȼƃɀȮŻȖ ȤȽ  ǌȮȽŸ ǠƶȮƸƭɀȮƶȾů ǞŽ .ȸȤȢȖ ƾž” ǝȼȮƴɀȮƴȼȮǬ ǀƱƸƴƒȘ
.ȵȼǎȮƸȮ ȾŶ ǜȾž ǝ ȼǧȼǍȮȾǬ ȶ ǀƱƸƴƒȚ ȚȼȤȼȶ ȠȚȤ .ǀƲƁǋƑƾŮ ǝǆȮƪȼƵɀȮƄȾȮƁ ȼȢ ǀƱƸƴƒȚ ȯƾŵ ȦȚǞſ ǞŮȚ ȆȳǞƁ ǛȼȮż ǋȮȼƯȮȼŮ
ȒȵȼǍ ɀƫȼȮŻ ɀț ƞƶȮȾžƻȮȽƓȚ ǍƸžȖ ȨȽǍɀȮǦȮȽƁ ȤȼǋȮɀȮǦȾȮƁ ǞƶȮȾž .ƿȮ ǆƆȮȼƯɀȮůȾȶ ǎȮȼƵȽȮǬ ǀƱƸƴƒȘ
 ȨȽǍɀȮǦȽȮů ȖǆǍȮ ǆƆȮȾů ȴǞƴɀȮŵ ȸƾƀ” ǝȼȮƴɀȮƴȼȮǬ .ȦȚǞſ ǞŮȚ ȯƾŵ Ȇǝ ȼǧȼǍȮȾǬ ǞƶȮȾž ȯǞƪȮɀƁ ȼȢ ȤȚǋɀȮſȘ ǀƱƸƴƒȘ
“!ȒƞƶȾžƻȮȽƓȚ ǍƸžȖ
“.ȜȼǋɀƸȮȼŮȽȥ ǁſȘ ȝȾǍǆȮƳȼȮź Ǡſȕ ȆȸǽɀǞȮȼž ǞƱȮȼƯɀȮŽȘ” ǝȼȮƴɀȮƴȼȮǬ ȦȚǞſ ǞŮȚ
 ǠƄȮɀűȾȶȼȥ ȨȽǍɀȮǦȽȮů ȖǆǍȮ ǆƆȮȾů ȴǞƴɀȮŵ” ǝȼȮƴɀȮƴȼȮǬ ȶ .ǝȼȮƄȮɀűȾȶȼȥ Ǡƀ ȜȼǋɀƸȮȼŮȽȥ ȴǈ ǍȼȮƅɀȮżȖ ƿ ȼƬȮȾŹ ǀƱƸƴƒȘ
“!ȒƞƶȾžƻȮȽƓȚ ȳȽȚ Ȇ ȜȼǋɀƸȮȾŮȽȥ
 ǁȾȮƃȼȮƴ ȾŶ ǍǆȮżǆ  ǌȮȾů ǁſȘ .ȸȤǌȮȽŸ Țȣƾƀ ǏȮȼŮ ȆȜȼǋɀƸȮȾŮȽȥ ȨȽǍɀȮǬȖ ȖǆǍȮǆűȖ ƾž Ǡſȕ ȆȸǽɀǞȮȼž” ǝȼȮƴɀȮƴȼȮǬ ȦȚǞſ ǞŮȚ
“ȒƿȮȾſǆ ǌȮŽȚ ǜȮɀž ǉȼƃɀȮŻȖ ȤȽ ǌȮȽŸ ǙƸƭɀȮſȖ ǠƦȮƶȮȾž
.ȦȚǞſ ǞŮȚ ǜȮȼŸ ƾȼȮƱȮȾŸȶ ȆǝȼƁȚǞȮɀƀ ǙȮ ȼƇ ȾǤ ǋƸȮŵȼǆǍȮŽȚ ȴȶȤƾƀ ǀƱƸƴƒȘ
Translation: “The excuse is worse than the misdeed.”
Once upon a time, the Caliph Harun ir-Rashid was strolling in his palace garden.
He saw Abu Nuwas in the garden drinking wine.
The Caliph was very angry because he was the Prince of the Believers.
When Abu Nuwas saw the Caliph he was afraid and hid the wine bottle behind his back.
The Caliph asked Abu Nuwas, “What are you hiding behind your back?”
Abu Nuwas said to him, “A bottle of water my lord.”
The Caliph said to him, “Let me see it.”
Abu Nuwas showed the bottle to the Caliph.
The Caliph saw the bottle with red wine in it and said to him, “How come the water is red?”
Abu Nuwas said to him, “My lord, when the water saw you it became abashed and turned red.”
The Caliph was very angry with Abu Nuwas and threatened him with death.
But Abu Nuwas begged the Caliph to forgive him.
The Caliph said to him, “I would forgive you on one condition: that you give me an excuse that is 
worse than the misdeed.”
Abu Nuwas said to him, “My lord, how could I give you an excuse that is worse than the 
misdeed?”

 |  l e s s o n  
The Caliph said “I don’t know. Either you give me an excuse that is worse than the misdeed or I 
shall kill you.”
A few days went by, Abu Nuwas saw the Caliph walking in the garden. He went behind him 
and pinched him on his buttocks.
The Caliph jumped in surprise and wondered who would to do that to him in his palace.
When the Caliph turned around to see who pinched him, he saw Abu Nuwas. He said to him, 
“How dare you pinch the Prince of the Believers?!”
Abu Nuwas said to him, “Sorry my lord, I thought you were Zubayda.”
The Caliph became even angrier because Zubayda was his wife, and said to him, “How dare you 
pinch my wife Zubayda, the Mother of the Believers?!”
Abu Nuwas said, “My lord, I don’t dare pinch Zubayda, but this is my excuse. Do you remember 
your demand from me that I give you an excuse that is worse than the misdeed?”
The Caliph Harun ir-Rashid laughed and forgave Abu Nuwas.
 
(The well-known saying of this story, “The excuse is worse than the misdeed,” is cited 
when somebody makes a mistake, doesn’t perform his/her duty etc., then gives a stupid 
excuse for the misdeed.)

2. The Story of the Lion and the Wolf
Title: “sh-saarat khoo muu farwat sabi9?”
fad yoom is-sabi (Abu Khmuyyis) malik il-haywaanaat chaan kullish at shaan w 
da-ydawwir ala mayy.
shaaf biir mayy. lamman baawa bil-biir shaaf dhiib waagi bil-biir.
is-sabi sallam adh-dhiib. widh-dhiib jaawaba “ahlan w sahlan yaa Abu Khmuyyis.”
is-sabi galla “sh-da-ssawwi jawwa bil-biir?”
idh-dhiib jaawaba “aani da-asawwi farwa.”
is-sabi galla “yaa dhiib, ij-jaw baarid bish-shita, aani ariid farwa hamm.”
idh-dhiib galla “zeen, asawwilak farwa, bass rah ahtaaj jilid tili”.
is-sabi raah w rija baad saaa saayid tiliyyeen smaan. w rima it-tiliyyeen lidh-dhiib 
bil-biir.
idh-dhiib galla “bass haadha muu kaaﬁ ahtaaj tilyaan hwaaya. tili waahid loo thneen 
bil-yoom.”
is-sabi gaam kull yoom yjiib tili loo tiliyyeen lidh-dhiib. w kull marra yis¢al “khilsat 
il-farwa?”
idh-dhiib yjaawba “ahtaaj baad jilid”.
badeen is-sabi khilas sabra, li¢ann kull marra yjiib tili, idh-dhiib yintii udhur jidiid 
“chinit mariid, chaan aku iid.”
bass Abu Khmuyyis zihag, w gaal lidh-dhiib “laazim tkhallis il-farwa!”
idh-dhiib jaawaba “baad yoomeen”.
is-sabi rija baad yoomeen, bass idh-dhiib galla “il-ubra inkisrat, taaal baachir w jiib 
habil wiyyaak.”
is-sabi riji thaani yoom w nazzal il-habil bil-biir hatta yis hab il-farwa.
is-sabi bidda yis hab il-farwa, w fakkar il-farwa laazim zeena li¢annha chaanat thigiila.
bass huwwa ma-chaan yidri idh-dhiib mitallig bil-habil wi-mghatti raasa b-jilid tili.
awwal-ma wisal idh-dhiib l-haaﬀat il-biir, rima ij-jilid ala wijih is-sabi wi-nhizam.
lamman Abu Khmuyyis shaal ij-jilid min ala wijha, idh-dhiib saar biiid w nija b-nafsa.
“ȒǕȾȮƃȮ ȼŴ ȝȼȶɀǍȼȮź ǞȮȽž ǞȮȽų ȝȼȤƾƫ ɀŵ” :ȴȚǞɀȮƶȮȽŸ
. ȿǠȼž ǟƴŸ ȤƦȶȼǋȮɀƁ ȿȢ ȶ ȴƾƪɀȮƭȼŸ ǐƦȮƴȽȮż ȴƾǩ ȝƾſȚǞɀƸȼƑȚ ǙȮȾƴȼž (ǏƦƸȮȼƵȽȮų ǞŮȚ) ǕȾȮƃȮ ǆƉŽȚ ȳǞƁ ǋȼȮź
.ǍƸƃɀȮŽƾȾȮŮ ǕȮȾǬȚȶ ƿȮƁȾȣ ȯƾŵ ǍȮƸƃɀȮŽƾȾȮŮ ȬȼȶƾŮ ǜȮǆƵȮȾŽ . ȿǠȼž ǍƸŮ ȯƾŵ
“.ǏƦƸȮȼƵȽȮų ǞŮȖ ƾƁ ǾɀƷ ȼŴȶ ǾɀƀȖ” ǝȼŮȼȶƾű ƿƁƦ  ǌȮŽȚȶ .ƿƁƦ ǌȮŽȚ ȼȬ ǛǆȮƴ ȼŴ ǕȾȮƃȮ ǆƉŽȘ
“ȒǍȮƸƃɀȮŽƾȾȮŮ ȸȿǞȮ ǆŴȼǋɀȮŵ” ǝȼȮƴɀȮƴȼȮǬ ǕȾȮƃȮ ǆƉŽȘ
“.ȜȼȶɀǍȼȮź ȸƦǞȮ ȼŴȚȢ Ǡſȕ” ǝȼŮȼȶƾű ƿƁƦ ǌȮŽȘ
“.ɀǛȮȼƀ ȜȼȶɀǍȼȮź ǋƁȤȖ Ǡſȕ ȆƾƄƦȮƪŽƾŮ ȢȤƾŮ ȿǞȮǆƐȚ ȆƿȮƁȣ ƾƁ” ǝȼȮƴɀȮƴȼȮǬ Ǖƃ ǆƉŽȘ
“.Ǡƴ ȾŶ ǋȮȾƴȾȮű ȟƾƄȮɀŲȖ ȠȼȤ ǏȮȼŮ ȆǜƁȥ” ǝȼȮƴɀȮƴȼȮǬ ƿƁƦ ǌȮŽȚ
.ǍƸƃŽƾŮ ƿƁǆ  ǌȮƴȮȾŽ ǜȮƸȮƦƸȮȾƴƦȮƭŽȚ ǟžȾȤȶ .ȴƾƵȮ ɀŴ ƞȮƦƸȾȮƴ ȾŶ ǝȼȮŽɀǋȾȮƁƾǧ ǀŸƾŴ ǋȮȼƯȮȼŮ ǕȼűȾȤ ȶ ȠȚȤ ǕȾȮƃȮ ǆƉŽȘ
“.ȳǞƸɀȮŽƾȾȮŮ ǜȮƸȮƦƸȾȮƴ ȾŶ ǞŽ ǋȾŲȚȶ Ǡƴ ȾŶ ȟƾƄɀŲȚ ȆǠźƾż ǞȽž Țȣƾƀ ǏȼŮ” ǝȼȮƴɀȮƴȼȮǬ ƿƁƦ ǌȮŽȘ

“!ȜȼȶɀǍȼȮƱŽȚ ǑƦȮƴȼȮƈɀȮů ȳȾȥǽ” ƿƁȼ ǌȮƴȮȾŽ ȲƾȮǬȶ ȆǙȮȼƀȾȥ ǏƦƸȮȽƵɀȮų ǞŮȚ ǏȼŮ
“.ƞžǞȮƁ ǋȼƯȮȼŮ” ǝȮȼŮȼȶƾű ƿƁȿ ǌȮŽȘ
 ǚȾȮƃȮȼŲ ƿƸűȶ ǍȾȮǩƾŮ ȲƾƯȼȮů ȆȝȼǍ ɀƉȮȾƳɀȮſȘ ȜȼǍɀŮǈȚ” ǝȼȮƴɀȮƴȼȮǬ ƿƁȿ ǌȮŽȚ ǏȼŮ ȆƞžǞƁ ǋƯŮ ǕűȤ Ǖƃ ǆƉŽȚ
“.ȱƾȮȿƁȾȶ
.ȜȼȶɀǍȼȮƱŽȚ ƿ ȼƇ ɀƉȾȮƁ ǟȿȮƄȼŲ ǍƸƃŽƾŮ ǚȾȮƃȼƑȚ ȲǆǎȼȮſȶ ȳǞƁ ǠſƾŰ ǕȼűȾȤ Ǖƃ ǆƉŽȘ
.ǀƴƸȮȾǦȮȾŰ ǁſƾǩ ƾƷɀȮſǈ ǀƶƁȥ ȳȾȥǽ ȜȼȶɀǍȼȮƱŽȚǍǆȮƳȼȮźȶ ȆȜȼȶɀǍȼȮƱŽȚ ƿ ȼƇ ɀƉȾȮƁ ȚȼǋȾȮŮ ǕƃȮ ǆƉŽȘ
.Ǡƴ ȾŶ ǋƴȾȮƆȾȮŮ ǝȮ ȼŴȚȤ ǠƦȮƭȼȮưȮɀžȾȶ ǚȾȮƃȮȼƑƾŮ ƩƦȮƴȼƯɀȮƄȾž ƿƁƦ ǌȮŽȚ ȸȤɀǋȾȮƁ ȴƾǩ ȼȳ Ǟƀ ǏȼŮ
.ȳȼǎȾȮƷɀȮſȾȶ ǕȾȮƃ ǆƉŽȚ ǝȾȮűȾȶ ǟƴŸ ǋȮȾƴȾȮƐȚ ǟžȾȤ ȆǍȮƸƃɀȮŽȚ ǀǆȮźƾŲ ƿƁƦ ǌŽȚ ǚ ȼǧȾȶ ȼȳ ȲǆȶȖ
.ǝ ȼƉɀȮƱȼȮſ ɀț ǟ Ⱦƍȶ ǋƸƯȾȮŮ Ȥƾǧ ƿƁƦ ǌȮŽȚ ȆǝȮȼƷȮɀűȾȶ ǟƴŸ ǜȾž ǋȾȮƴȮƦƐȚ Ȳƾŵ ǏƦƸȮȽƵɀȮų ǞŮȚ ǜȮǆƵȼȮŽ
Translation: “What happened? It’s not the lion’s fur coat!”
One day, the lion (Abu Khmuyyis), the king of the beasts, was very thirsty looking for water.
He saw a well. When he looked down into the well he saw a wolf.
The lion greeted the wolf and the wolf said to him, “Welcome Abu Khmuyyis.”
Said the lion, “What are you doing down there?”
The wolf replied, “I am making a fur coat.”
The lion said, “O wolf, the winter is cold and I would like to have a fur coat, too.”
Said the wolf, “Okay, but I will need sheepskins.”
The lion went and came back after one hour having hunted two fat sheep and threw them down 
the well to the wolf.
The wolf said, “But this is not enough. I shall need one or two sheep every day.”
The lion began to bring one or two lambs every day to the wolf, and asked him each time, “Is the 
fur coat done?”
The wolf would respond “I need more skin.”
Then the lion ran out of patience because each time he brought a lamb, the wolf gave him new 
excuses for not ﬁnishing the coat, “I was ill, there was a feast,” etc.
But the lion got fed up and said to the wolf, “You must ﬁnish the coat!”
Said the wolf, “In two days.”
When the lion returned after two days, the wolf said to him, “The needle broke. Come tomorrow 
and bring a rope with you.”
The lion came the next day and threw the rope down the well to pull up the fur coat.
As he began to pull up the coat he thought it must be a ﬁne one since it was heavy.
But he did not realize that the wolf was hanging on the rope after covering his head with a 
sheepskin.

 |  l e s s o n  
As soon as the wolf was drawn to top of the well, he threw the skins in the lion’s face and ﬂed.
By the time Abu Khmuyyis lifted the skins oﬀ his face the wolf was far away and saved himself.
 
(The saying of this story: “What happened? It’s not the lion’s fur coat!” is cited when 
delivering a job, especially if a garment is postponed a few times—E.S. Stevens, Folk-Tales 
of Iraq, Oxford University Press, London .)

Grammar and Remarks
Conjunctions:
Conjunctions are used to connect words, phrases or clauses, e.g. and, or, if, etc. Several con-
junctions have already appeared in the book. There are two types of conjunctions in Iraqi 
Arabic: simple conjunctions and those which are formed by adding the suﬃx “-ma” to cer-
tain prepositions, interrogatives, or nouns. Below is a list of the most common conjunctions 
of the two types with examples. (Audio)

 |  l e s s o n  

Laylaa iraaqiyyiin

2. Conjunctions with Suﬃx -ma ȼȳ:
a. With Interrogatives:
ween? (where?)

wiyyaak (Wherever you go, I

(I want the car, no matter

 ȼȳ ǋȼȮƳ ȼŵ ȜȤƾƸȮ ǆƉŽȚ ǋƁȤȖ

țƾƄɀȮƳȮȾŽ ǌȽȮųƾƁ ǠƆȾȮƁ ȼȳ ǞƶȮȾž

Idioms and Common Phrases (Audio)
1. makaan (ak) khaali ǠŽƾų ǙȼȮſƾƳȼž 
(You) were missed. (lit. Your place was vacant.)

We missed you.

2. 9aashat iid + pronoun 
+ǋȮƁȘ ǁȼȮŵƾŸ 
well done (lit. long live (your) hand.)
It is said to someone who has done a good job.
aashat iidak haadha

6. Translate the following into Arabic:
Whenever I go to the school garden I see Samar there.
 
Iraq is one of the most ancient countries in the world.
 
Mesopotamia means the land between the two rivers, the Tigris and the Euphrates.
 
Harun ir-Rashid was the most famous caliph of the Islamic Empire.
 

 |  l e s s o n  
D R I L L S
The “Epic of Gilgamesh” is the oldest literary tale; it preceded Homer’s Iliad by more than 
two thousand years.
 
Harun ir-Rashid, Prince of the Believers, was married to Zubayda, Mother of the Believers.
 
Was Abu Nuwas a well-known poet or a musician in the caliph’s palace?
 
The lion is stronger than the wolf, but the wolf is smarter than he is.
 
The wolf had fallen in the well and he tricked the lion to take him out of the well.
 
The wolf lied to the lion because he wanted to save himself.
 
The lion ran out of patience after many excuses from the wolf.
 
Better to have a good brain than strength without a brain.
 
Whenever I go home I ﬁnd my sister watching TV.
 
After I study Arabic here, I shall go to the University of Cairo in Egypt for two years.
 
I would have gone to Baghdad if I had spoken Iraqi Arabic better.
 
What have you learned from these two old folktales?
 
7. Write two short paragraphs: one telling about an experience you might have had similar 
to the folktale, “The excuse is worse than the misdeed,” and the second similar to the 
folktale, “What happened? It’s not the lion’s fur coat.”

Please read the notes below.
This glossary contains the words, expressions, phrases, and idioms that occur in this book. 
It is organized as follows:
 
Both the Arabic–English and the English–Arabic sections are arranged alphabetically. 
The Arabic–English section is listed according to the Iraqi Arabic letters and symbols, 
which are shown in lesson  (¢ Ȕ, b ț, p Ɂ, t ȝ, th Ȟ, j ȟ, ch Ƀ, h Ƞ, kh ȡ, d Ȣ, dh ȣ, r Ȥ, z ȥ, 
s Ȧ, sh ȧ, s Ȩ, d ȩ, t Ȫ, z ȫ,  Ȭ, gh ȭ, g Ɇ, f ȯ, q Ȱ, k ȱ, l Ȳ, l Ȳ, m ȳ, n ȴ, h Ȯƀ, w ȶ, 
y ȸ). The English–Arabic section is listed in the English alphabetical order.
 . A cross-reference system is used in the glossary. References are made to the relevant 
discussion, structure, or grammar either in the text or to another heading in the glos-
sary. A word that occurs or constitutes part of a common phrase/idiom is listed under 
that word.
 . Since the glottal stop sound, the so-called hamza (¢), is not rendered graphically in this 
text when it occurs in the beginning of the word, the reader will ﬁnd the words that 
begin with one of the vowels, “a,” “i,” or “u” listed in that vowel order under the heading 
(¢) in the glossary.
 . Verbs are listed, as usual, under the third person masculine singular form (he) in the past 
tense followed by the present tense.
 . Nouns and adjectives (including relative adjectives) are listed without the deﬁnite arti-
cle “il-” in their singular forms followed by the plural, masculine, and feminine forms.
